Find more information on<a href="https://help.altmetric.com/support/solutions/articles/6000233311-how-is-the-altmetric-attention-score-calculated"> the Altmetric Attention Score and how the score is calculated.</a></p></div></div></div></div></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="_i1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c17466/asset/images/medium/am1c17466_0010.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c17466/asset/images/large/am1c17466_0010.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.1c17466&amp;id=_i1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">As a promising alternative to lithium-ion batteries (LIBs), rechargeable sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are attracting enormous attention due to the abundance of sodium. However, the lack of high-performance sodium anode materials limits the commercialization of SIBs. In this work, the dual enhancement of SnS<sub>2</sub>/graphene anodes in sodium storage is achieved through S-compositing and Co doping via an innovative one-step hydrothermal reaction at a relatively low temperature of 120 °C. The as-prepared 7% Co-SnS<sub>2</sub>/S@r-G composite consisting of 15.4 wt % S and 1.49 atom % Co shows both superior cycling stability (over 1000 cycles) and rate capability, giving high reversible specific capacities of 878, 608, and 470 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.2, 5, and 10 A g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. More encouragingly, the full-cell also exhibits an outstanding long-term cycling performance under 0.5 A g<sup>–1</sup>, which delivers a reversible capacity of 500 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> over 200 cycles and still retains a high reversible capacity of 432 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> over 400 cycles. The enhancement mechanism is attributed to the favorable three-dimensional structure of the composite, Co doping, and S-composition, which can induce a synergistic effect.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D54043%26pageCount%3D16%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DLu%2BYue%252C%2BKai%2BLi%252C%2BGengzhi%2BSun%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D13%26issueNum%3D45%26contentID%3Dacsami.1c17466%26title%3DDual%2BEnhancement%2Bof%2BSodium%2BStorage%2BInduced%2Bthrough%2BBoth%2BS-Compositing%2Band%2BCo-Doping%2BStrategies%26numPages%3D16%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1944-8244%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Daamick%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D54058%26publicationDate%3DNovember%2B2021" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2021 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">
                                        Article subjects are automatically applied from the ACS Subject Taxonomy and describe the scientific concepts and themes of the article.</p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291445&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Chemical structure"> Chemical structure </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291331&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Composites"> Composites </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=290714&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Doping"> Doping </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291371&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrodes"> Electrodes </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292522&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Two dimensional materials"> Two dimensional materials </a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--keywords hlFld-Abstract"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Keywords</h3><div class="article__tags__tooltip" tabindex="0"><div class="article__tags__icon" aria-describedby="tooltip-keywords"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are keywords</span></div><p class="article__tags__diff" id="tooltip-keywords" role="tooltip">
                        Article keywords are supplied by the authors and highlight key terms and topics
                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Transition+Metal+Sulfides&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_transition%20metal%20sulfides">transition metal sulfides</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sns2&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_SnS2">SnS<sub>2</sub></a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Graphene%2C+S-composites&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_graphene%2C%20S-composites">graphene, S-composites</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Co+Doping%2C+Dual+Enhancement&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_Co%20doping%2C%20dual%20enhancement">Co doping, dual enhancement</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodium-ion+Batteries&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodium-ion%20batteries">sodium-ion batteries</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Anodes&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_anodes">anodes</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">1.  Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">Rechargeable batteries such as lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) and sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have been recognized as efficient energy-storage devices for electrical energy storage and conversion. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref1 ref2" aria-label="reference 1 and 2">(1,2)</a> Comparably, SIBs have the advantages of a much more rich natural abundance of sodium and low cost than lithium and are considered as promising alternatives to LIBs. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref3" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref3" aria-label="reference 3">(3)</a> However, the large radius of Na<sup>+</sup> typically leads to a slow diffusion rate and large volume change of the electrode materials during charging and discharging processes, degrading the performance of SIBs. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref4" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref4" aria-label="reference 4">(4)</a> Therefore, exploring new materials for sodium storage has become one of the cutting-edge research areas in recent years. Among all potential candidates, transition metal dichalcogenides (TMDs) are identified as promising anode materials for SIBs because of (1) a high theoretical capacity induced by their multielectron transfer mechanism, (2) a layered structure with a high specific surface area and shortened diffusion distance of Na<sup>+</sup>, and (3) a low volume effect due to the weak van der Waals interlayer force and large spacing. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref5 ref6" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref5 ref6" aria-label="reference 5 and 6">(5,6)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">As a typical TMD, tin disulfide (SnS<sub>2</sub>) has attracted great attention due to its layered CdI<sub>2</sub> crystalline-like structure, large interlayer spacing (5.90 Å in (001) plane) for the reversible crossing of Na<sup>+</sup> (size: 1.02Å), super high theoretical specific capacity (Na<sub>15</sub>Sn<sub>4</sub>: 1136 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>), and suitable redox potential. However, SnS<sub>2</sub> remains suffering from ∼324% volume change during sodiation/desodiation (leading to crushing and peeling off), insufficient ionic/electronic conductivity, and sluggish reaction kinetics, which restricts their practical applications in SIBs. Hybridizing SnS<sub>2</sub> with carbonaceous materials is usually used to alleviate the electrode volume expansion and improve conductivity. Comparably, graphene featuring a two-dimensional nanostructure, excellent electronic conductivity, ultrahigh specific surface area, and high mechanical strength can effectively enhance the electronic conductivity and the electrical contact of the active electrode nanoparticles when it is used as a matrix. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref7" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref7" aria-label="reference 7">(7)</a> In this regard, a variety of SnS<sub>2</sub>-graphene composites have been explored as the anodes of SIBs. For instance, the free-standing SnS<sub>2</sub>/graphene electrode prepared by the dissolution-regeneration strategy displayed a reversible capacity of 300 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> under 2 A g<sup>–1</sup> over 400 cycles. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref8" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref8" aria-label="reference 8">(8)</a> The ultrathin SnS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets/N-doped graphene composites synthesized by a thermal decomposition method showed a reversible capacity of 453 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> under 0.5 A g<sup>–1</sup> over 200 cycles. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref9" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref9" aria-label="reference 9">(9)</a> The SnS<sub>2</sub> nanoplatelet@graphene composite, which is hydrothermally obtained, delivered a specific capacity of 725 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> under 20 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref10" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref10" aria-label="reference 10">(10)</a> The SnS<sub>2</sub>/N,S-doped graphene composite reported by Fan et al. delivered specific capacities of 527 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> upon 50 cycles and 340 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> under 800 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref11" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref11" aria-label="reference 11">(11)</a> The SnS<sub>2</sub>@C@rGO electrode reported by Li et al. delivered reversible capacities of 722 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> under 0.05 A g<sup>–1</sup> and 397 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> under 2 A g<sup>–1</sup>. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref12" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref12" aria-label="reference 12">(12)</a> However, it remains challenging to achieve good rate performance and long cycle-life at the same time, especially in the process of deep charge/discharge under high current density.</div><div class="NLM_p">Metal doping is another effective strategy to enhance the electrochemical performance of SnS<sub>2</sub>. For example, metal dopants (e.g., Fe, Co, Ni, Mn, and Mg) can effectively increase the layer spacing of SnS<sub>2</sub>, narrow the band gap, enrich the lattice defects, and improve the electronic conductivity. In fact, such a doping strategy has been widely used to enhance the performance in various applications, such as solar cells, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref13" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref13" aria-label="reference 13">(13)</a> electromagnetic fields, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref14" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref14" aria-label="reference 14">(14)</a> Li-S batteries, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref15" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref15" aria-label="reference 15">(15)</a> and LIBs, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref16" aria-label="reference 16">(16)</a> yet seldom reported in the field of sodium storage. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref17" aria-label="reference 17">(17)</a></div><div class="NLM_p last">In this work, improved sodium storage performance is successfully achieved for SnS<sub>2</sub>/graphene anodes through hybridizing with S and introducing Co doping via a one-step hydrothermal process at 120 °C without the addition of any organic solvents or the involvement of postannealing. The hydrothermal reaction process controlled by the medium-low temperature liquid phase is simple and does not need high-temperature treatment. This is of great practical significance to promote the low-energy preparation of high-performance two-dimensional homogeneous composites. The performance-enhancing mechanisms were investigated, and the contributions of sulfur and Co doping were highlighted. To the best of our knowledge, the obtained Co-SnS<sub>2</sub>/S@r-G gives better Na<sup>+</sup> storage performance and superior rate capability as anode materials in SIBs.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i3">2.  Experiment</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec2_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i4" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i4">2.1.  Sample Preparation</h3><div class="NLM_p last">First, 0.1 g of SnCl<sub>4</sub>·5H<sub>2</sub>O was dispersed in 20 mL of deionized water by ultrasound until it was completely dissolved; after being stirred for 10 min, 20 mL of aqueous dispersion of 0.8 mg mL<sup>–1</sup> graphene oxide (GO) was added under ultrasound for 30 min to form a mixed solution. Then, CoCl<sub>2</sub>·6H<sub>2</sub>O with a doping molar ratio of 3, 7, 9, 11, or 13% (relative to SnCl<sub>4</sub>·5H<sub>2</sub>O) and 0.1 g of thioacetamide were ultrasonically dissolved in the above mixed solution with 2 mL of concentrated HCl slowly dripped under stirring. Finally, the formed mixed solution was transferred to a Teflon-lined autoclave and heated at 120 °C for 24 h. The as-synthesized product was finally obtained after washing and drying in a vacuum at 70 °C for 3 h, and the samples are denoted as <i>x</i>% Co-SnS<sub>2</sub>/S@r-G with <i>x</i> = 0, 3, 5, 7, 9, 11, and 13. For comparison, 7% Co-doped samples were also prepared during the process without adding hydrochloric acid (named as 7% Co-SnS<sub>2</sub>@r-G), and different reaction temperatures at 100, 150, and 180 °C with the similar conditions described above (named as 7% Co-SnS<sub>2</sub>@r-G(100, 150, or 180)), respectively. It should be noted that the reduced graphene (r-G) was prepared by a hydrothermal reaction of GO solution at 120 °C for 24 h and then sintered in Ar gas at 450 °C. Furthermore, to study the effect of sulfur on a composite, a 7% Co-SnS<sub>2</sub>/S@r-G sample was annealed at 400 and 600 °C under an Ar atmosphere, named as 7% Co-SnS<sub>2</sub>@r-G(400 or 600).</div></div><div id="sec2_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i5" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i5">2.2.  Characterization of the Samples</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The morphologies of the prepared samples were characterized using a scanning electron microscope (SEM, Nova NanoSEM 450) and a transmission electron microscope (TEM and HRTEM, JEM-2100F) equipped with an energy-dispersive spectroscopy (EDS) detector to measure the composition and element distribution of the samples. The structures were investigated via X-ray diffraction (XRD, XʼPert3 Powder) and X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS, ESCALAB 250Xi). Raman spectroscopy was performed using a HORIBA Jobin Yvon (France) with 532 nm diode laser excitation. The thermal gravimetric analysis (TGA) was performed with a NETZSCH STA 449C analyzer (Germany) in air flow with a heating rate of 10 °C min<sup>–1</sup>. The Brunauer–Emmett–Teller (BET) was measured using a Beckman Coulter SA3100. The electrochemical performances were performed with a Zennium/IM6 electrochemical workstation (Germany). The cycling performance measurement was performed at a Shenzhen Neware battery cycler (China).</div></div><div id="sec2_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i6" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i6">2.3.  Characterization by Electrochemical Measurements</h3><div class="NLM_p last">For sample paste preparation, the sample was mixed with carboxymethyl cellulose and acetylene black in a weight ratio of 3:1:1 to form a homogeneous paste mixture. This mixture was coated onto a Cu foil and dried at 70 °C under vacuum for 6 h, then punched into small circular electrodes. The loading amounts of active materials on the electrodes are about 1.0–1.2 mg cm<sup>–2</sup>. The CR2025 coin cells served as the test half-cells, which were composed of the circular electrodes (working electrodes), Na foil (counter electrode), and glass fiber (separator, Whatman, GF/C) with the injection of drops of 1 M NaClO<sub>4</sub> EC/DEC/DMC (1:1:1 by volume) with a 5% fluoroethylene carbonate additive as the electrolyte.</div></div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i7">3.  Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec3_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i8" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i8">3.1.  Morphology/Structure and Composition Characterization</h3><div class="NLM_p">The preparation steps of the Co-doped SnS<sub>2</sub>/S@graphene composite are schematically shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>. At the beginning of the reaction process, thioacetamide is first hydrolyzed to generate a large amount of H<sub>2</sub>S under acidic and heating conditions. The H<sub>2</sub>S reacts with Sn<sup>4+</sup> adsorbed on the GO surface to generate SnS<sub>2</sub>. Then, the SnS<sub>2</sub> reacts with the oxygen-contained functional groups of the GO surface. In this step, GO is deoxygenated to the reduced GO (r-G) and S<sup>2–</sup> is transformed into a single S. Meanwhile, the generated S can also react with Sn<sup>4+</sup> to generate SnS<sub>2</sub>. Co doping takes place synchronously in the formation process of SnS<sub>2</sub>, and a composite with Co-doped SnS<sub>2</sub> and single S attached to the GO layers is obtained.</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c17466/asset/images/medium/am1c17466_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c17466/asset/images/large/am1c17466_0002.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. Schematic illustration of the preparation procedures for S-composited and Co-doped SnS<sub>2</sub>/graphene anode materials of sodium-ion batteries (SIBs).</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c17466/asset/images/large/am1c17466_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.1c17466&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The crystal structures of the samples were confirmed by XRD analysis, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 2</a>a and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c17466/suppl_file/am1c17466_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S1a,b</a>. A broad diffraction peak at about 25° for the r-G sample can be assigned to the (002) plane of graphene, indicating the restacking of graphene layers in the synthesis process. In comparison with r-G, there is a strong, broad diffraction peak at about 23° for the r-G/S sample, which can be assigned to the existence of noncrystalline state S in/on multigraphene layers. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref18" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref18" aria-label="reference 18">(18)</a> The XRD pattern of the SnS<sub>2</sub> sample matches well with the hexagonal SnS<sub>2</sub> (JCPDS 23-0677). For <i>x</i>% Co-SnS<sub>2</sub>/S@r-G samples, four obvious diffraction peaks at about 17, 23, 28, and 50° can be observed clearly, which can be assigned to the SnS<sub>2</sub> (JCPDS 23-0677) and S (JCPDS 08-0247). For <i>x</i>% Co-SnS<sub>2</sub>/S@r-G samples, the (100) and (110) peaks have the maximum intensities, indicating the strong (100) and (110) preferential growth of the samples. Meanwhile, no diffraction peak of cobalt sulfide can be found from the Co-doped SnS<sub>2</sub> samples, indicating that a low content of Co can be doped into the SnS<sub>2</sub> crystal lattice. Furthermore, in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c17466/suppl_file/am1c17466_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1a</a>, it can be found that the (100) and (110) peaks of <i>x</i>% Co-SnS<sub>2</sub>/S@r-G slightly shift toward a higher 2θ direction with the increase of <i>x</i> values. This implies that the low doping concentration of Co<sup>2+</sup> has substituted Sn<sup>4+</sup> in the as-prepared SnS<sub>2</sub> samples. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref19" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref19" aria-label="reference 19">(19)</a> For 7% Co-SnS<sub>2</sub>@r-G(400) and 7% Co-SnS<sub>2</sub>@r-G(600) samples in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c17466/suppl_file/am1c17466_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1b</a>, the diffraction peaks at 23° representing S are significantly decreased or even disappeared completely, while the peaks at 28 and 50° representing SnS<sub>2</sub> still existed. This phenomenon is related to the fact that sulfur is easy to sublimate under high temperatures. Simultaneously, for 7% Co-SnS<sub>2</sub>@r-G, 7% Co-SnS<sub>2</sub>/S@r-G(150), and 7% Co-SnS<sub>2</sub>/S@r-G(180) samples, the diffraction peaks at 23° representing S have also not been observed in the spectra. It can be inferred that the hydrochloric acid and reaction temperature play crucial roles in the formation of the sulfur complex structure in the process. In the absence of hydrochloric acid, thioacetamide cannot be hydrolyzed to produce H<sub>2</sub>S; therefore, it cannot react with GO to produce S. In the meantime, when the hydrothermal temperature is higher than 120 °C, the produced S has other side reactions and is dissolved from the graphene structure.</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c17466/asset/images/medium/am1c17466_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c17466/asset/images/large/am1c17466_0003.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. (a) XRD patterns, (b) S 2p XPS, (c) Raman shift curves of full spectra, (d) the magnified Raman shift curves for the A1<sub>g</sub> peak of SnS<sub>2</sub>, (e) TGA, and (f) BET curves of the as-prepared samples.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c17466/asset/images/large/am1c17466_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.1c17466&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The chemical composition and valence states of all samples were studied through XPS. <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c17466/suppl_file/am1c17466_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2a</a> shows the fully scanned spectra of the samples. The characteristic peaks of Sn 4d, S 2p<sub>3</sub>, S 2s, C 1s, Sn 3d, O 1s, Sn 3p<sub>3</sub>, Sn 3p<sub>1</sub>, Sn Auger, and C Auger appear at 24.0, 163.9, 229.0, 285.0, 485.0, 530.2, 714.6, 756.2, 1058.0, and 1221.4 eV, respectively, which can be observed clearly for all samples. Due to the low content of Co, its characteristic peaks cannot be seen in all spectra. The S/Sn atomic ratio and the Co atomic ratio (%) are given in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c17466/suppl_file/am1c17466_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2b</a>. For SnS<sub>2</sub>, the S/Sn atomic ratio is measured to be 1.92, which is close to the stoichiometry. For the 7% Co-SnS<sub>2</sub>@r-G and 7% Co-SnS<sub>2</sub>@r-G (150) samples, the S/Sn atomic ratio is 1.87 and 1.85, respectively. The decrease of the S/Sn ratio confirms that sulfur is deficient due to Co<sup>2+</sup> doping. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref20" aria-label="reference 20">(20)</a> For 7% Co-SnS<sub>2</sub>@r-G(400), a S/Sn atomic ratio of 2.06 is slightly higher than SnS<sub>2</sub> due to the incomplete removal of S by heat treatment. For other <i>x</i>% Co-SnS<sub>2</sub>/S@r-G samples, all of the S/Sn atomic ratios are greater than 2.6, which are much higher than the stoichiometric ratio of 2. This is another evidence of the S existence in the samples. The measured Co atomic ratios are found to be 0.69, 1.22, 1.36, 1.53, and 1.49 atom % for the SnS<sub>2</sub>/S@r-G composites synthesized with 3, 7, 9, 11, and 13% doping molar concentrations in the synthesis process, respectively. Generally, the Co-doping concentration increases with the increase of the Co molar ratio in the synthesis process. The Co content of 7% Co-SnS<sub>2</sub>/S@r-G (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c17466/suppl_file/am1c17466_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3 and Table S1</a>) was also measured by EDS. The measured Co atomic ratio and the weight ratio are 1.14 and 3.08%, respectively, which are very close to the XPS-tested results.</div><div class="NLM_p">In <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>b, S 2p peaks for SnS<sub>2</sub>/S@r-G and 7% Co-SnS<sub>2</sub>/S@r-G, S 2p<sub>3/2</sub>, S 2p<sub>1/2</sub>, S–S, S–C, and S–O show the band energy energies of 161.2, 162.4, 164.1, 164.8, and 168.1 eV, respectively. S 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and S 2p<sub>1/2</sub> bands can be ascribed to S<sup>2–</sup>, which agrees with the reference values of the SnS<sub>2</sub> crystal. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref21" aria-label="reference 21">(21)</a> The S–S band can be ascribed to S in the composite, while the S–C band implies that the S heteroatom tends to dope into the carbon matrix. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref22" aria-label="reference 22">(22)</a> The weak S–O band is attributed to the sulfate species due to the oxidation of S. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref23" aria-label="reference 23">(23)</a> For the 7% Co-SnS<sub>2</sub>@r-G sample, all bands, except for S–S, can be obtained by dividing the S 2p peaks. These observations are consistent with the above results, further indicating that S exists in the composites. For C 1s peaks shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c17466/suppl_file/am1c17466_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2d</a>, C–C, defect, C–S, and −O–C═O bands display 284.1, 285.5, 286.4, and 288.4 eV, respectively. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref24" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref24" aria-label="reference 24">(24)</a> For 7% Co-SnS<sub>2</sub>@r-G(150), 7% Co-SnS<sub>2</sub>@r-G, 7% Co-SnS<sub>2</sub>@r-G(400), 7% Co-SnS<sub>2</sub>/S@r-G, and SnS<sub>2</sub>/S@r-G, the contents of C–C and defective carbon in the carbon structure are 82.0 and 12.1%, 68.0 and 20.6%, 80.5 and 15.6%, 85.8 and 9.0%, and 78.8 and 16.0%, respectively. These results show that the carbon structure in graphene can be reorganized into a more ordered structure only by adding hydrochloric acid and a cobalt source at 120 °C in the reaction process without increasing the hydrothermal temperature and subsequent high-temperature annealing treatment. It should be noted that some metal ions such as cobalt are very effective catalysts for carbon atom reforming. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref25 ref26" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref25 ref26" aria-label="reference 25 and 26">(25,26)</a> The content of C–S in the carbon structure for 7% Co-SnS<sub>2</sub>/S@r-G is about 3.6%. It is known that doping with a small amount of heteroatom S can effectively improve both the electronic conductivity and sodium-ion storage performance for carbon materials. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref27" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref27" aria-label="reference 27">(27)</a> For the SnS<sub>2</sub>/S@r-G sample shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c17466/suppl_file/am1c17466_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2e</a>, two peaks of Sn 3d at 486.5 and 495.1 eV with an energy difference of 8.5 eV are similar to the Sn 3d<sub>5/2</sub> and Sn 3d<sub>3/2</sub> of Sn<sup>4+</sup>. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref11" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref11" aria-label="reference 11">(11)</a> However, the Sn 3d<sub>3/2</sub> and Sn 3d<sub>5/2</sub> peaks of Co-doped samples are shifted toward lower binding energies of about 0.4 eV compared with the above samples. This shift might be due to Co<sup>2+</sup> replacing Sn<sup>4+</sup> sites in the SnS<sub>2</sub> crystal lattice, thus leading to a change in the interaction of valence 3d electrons with the Sn core and enlarging the interlayer space. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref28 ref29" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref28 ref29" aria-label="reference 28 and 29">(28,29)</a><a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c17466/suppl_file/am1c17466_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2f</a> shows the Co 2p high-resolution region spectra. The peaks at 780.2 and 781.7 eV with a vibration satellite peak at 790.0 eV correspond to Co 2p<sub>3/2</sub>, and other peaks at 795.4 and 797.6 eV with a satellite peak at 805.0 eV correspond to Co 2p<sub>1/2</sub>. Among them, the signal peaks at 780.2 and 795.4 eV with a difference of 15.2 eV are assigned to the presence of Co<sup>2+</sup>, while the signal peaks at 781.7 and 797.6 eV with a difference of 15.9 eV can be assigned to the oxidized Co<sup>2+</sup>. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30 ref31" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref30 ref31" aria-label="reference 30 and 31">(30,31)</a> Therefore, cobalt is finally the form of Co<sup>2+</sup> doped in the samples. At the same time, the stronger signal peak at a 9% Co-SnS<sub>2</sub>/S@r-G sample than the SnS<sub>2</sub>/S@r-G, 3% Co-SnS<sub>2</sub>/S@r-G, and 7% Co-SnS<sub>2</sub>/S@r-G samples can be observed, which is strong evidence of the obviously higher cobalt content in the composite.</div><div class="NLM_p">Raman analysis is an important and effective method to study the crystal structure and the size effect of the nanostructured material. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>c, two intense peaks at 1350 and 1580 cm<sup>–1</sup> are attributed to the disorder-induced D band and graphitic-G band for r-G, respectively, while an obvious peak at 315 cm<sup>–1</sup> attributed to the A1<sub>g</sub> mode of the SnS<sub>2</sub> hexagonal phase can be observed in the SnS<sub>2</sub> spectrum. All of these characteristic peaks can be found in SnS<sub>2</sub>/r-G composite spectra. However, there is no obvious S-associated Raman vibration mode in r-G/S and other composites. The result is consistent with the research of Zhang et al. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref18" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref18" aria-label="reference 18">(18)</a> It is calculated that the <i>I</i><sub>G</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>D</sub> ratios for r-G and r-G/S are 0.94 and 0.70, respectively. The lower <i>I</i><sub>G</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>D</sub> ratio for r-G/S indicates a more disordered structure in carbon, which can be attributed to the sulfuration process and S doping. Meanwhile, it can be found that the <i>I</i><sub>G</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>D</sub> ratios for SnS<sub>2</sub>/S@r-G, 3% Co-SnS<sub>2</sub>/S@r-G, and 7% Co-SnS<sub>2</sub>/S@r-G increase from 0.72 to 0.76 and 0.89, showing an effective adjustment for the carbon structure disorder by appropriately doping with Co in the synthesis process (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c17466/suppl_file/am1c17466_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4</a>). These results are consistent with the above XPS results. For 7% Co-SnS<sub>2</sub>/S@r-G, the graphitization degree is still higher than others despite the sulfur doping process. It is known that the orderly graphitic carbon structure for graphene is beneficial to improve the electronic conduction of the composite. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>d shows the magnified Raman shift curves for the SnS<sub>2</sub> A1<sub>g</sub> peak, indicating that after Co doping, the A1<sub>g</sub> mode of SnS<sub>2</sub> tends to shift toward a lower wavenumber, which can be related to the distortion in the lattice induced by Co ions. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref32" aria-label="reference 32">(32)</a></div><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>e shows the TGA curves of the samples in an air atmosphere. The contents of graphene and S in composites can be determined by the curves. As shown in the curve of the SnS<sub>2</sub> sample, the large weight loss appearing above 400 °C corresponds to the slow oxidation of SnS<sub>2</sub> into SnO<sub>2</sub>, which can be expressed as SnS<sub>2</sub> + 3O<sub>2</sub> → SnO<sub>2</sub> +2SO<sub>2</sub>↑. The final residue of SnO<sub>2</sub> is 81.9% at 800 °C in the curve, which is close to the theoretical value of 82.4%. For the r-G/S sample, one huge weight loss between 200 and 550 °C can be attributed to the sulfur combustion, and another weight loss between 550 and 650 °C corresponds to the oxidation of carbon. For 7% Co-SnS<sub>2</sub>/S@r-G, three significant weight loss transitions occur, including the volatilization of adsorbed water, the decomposition of oxygen-containing functional groups, and the oxidation of the carbon structure, S and SnS<sub>2</sub>. The final residue is 59.7% at 800 °C in the curve. The 7% Co-SnS<sub>2</sub>@r-G sample displays a weight loss curve similar to SnS<sub>2</sub> with a final residue of 72.4% at 800 °C. According to the formula: SnS<sub>2</sub>% = SnO<sub>2</sub>% × 121.32%, the content of SnS<sub>2</sub> calculated by the above formula is 87.83 and 72.43% for 7% Co-SnS<sub>2</sub>/S@r-G and 7% Co-SnS<sub>2</sub>@r-G, respectively (neglecting the factor of a small amount of doped Co in SnS<sub>2</sub>). According to the results above, it can be concluded that the contents of graphene and S in the 7% Co-SnS<sub>2</sub>/S@r-G sample are about 12.2 and 15.4%, respectively. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 2</a>f and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c17466/suppl_file/am1c17466_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S5</a> show the N<sub>2</sub> adsorption–desorption isotherms of the samples. The r-G sample shows typical surface-layered adsorption on graphene ascribed to IUPAC type IV. SnS<sub>2</sub> displays a weak adsorbate–adsorbent interaction from macropores ascribed to particle agglomeration. The 7% Co-SnS<sub>2</sub>/S@r-G displays a much larger adsorption amount than that of 7% Co-SnS<sub>2</sub>@r-G and r-G/S and shows a typical IUPAC type V with a hysteresis loop above <i>P</i>/<i>P</i><sub>0</sub> = 0.45. This type of isotherm is mainly attributed to the multilayer adsorption with capillary condensation. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref33" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref33" aria-label="reference 33">(33)</a> Furthermore, 7% Co-SnS<sub>2</sub>/S@r-G displays a relatively higher surface area of 56.1 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup> and a total pore volume of 0.33 cm<sup>3</sup> g<sup>–1</sup> than 7% Co-SnS<sub>2</sub>@r-G (27.6 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup>, 0.15 cm<sup>3</sup> g<sup>–1</sup>) and r-G/S (18.6 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup>, 0.12 cm<sup>3</sup> g<sup>–1</sup>).</div><div class="NLM_p">The morphology of the samples was also investigated by SEM. For SnS<sub>2</sub> shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c17466/suppl_file/am1c17466_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6a</a>, a large agglomerate of many nanoparticles appears. For the r-G/S sample in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>a, a translucent sheet structure can be found. <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c17466/suppl_file/am1c17466_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6b–e</a> shows the SEM images of <i>x</i>% Co-SnS<sub>2</sub>/S@r-G samples, all of these exhibits a uniform but opaque sheet-like structure. For 7% Co-SnS<sub>2</sub>@r-G shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>c, it has a larger and more complete lamellar structure without the wrinkled piece compared with <i>x</i>% Co-SnS<sub>2</sub>/S@r-G and r-G/S samples. This may be due to the absence of HCl in the synthesis process and a large amount of hydrogen sulfide gas to participate in the full reduction of the GO surface. As shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c17466/suppl_file/am1c17466_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6h,i</a>, 7% Co-SnS<sub>2</sub>@r-G(150) and 7% Co-SnS<sub>2</sub>@r-G(180) present a curly sheet structure with some regular sheets and accumulations to form a composite structure. The regular sheets and their accumulations can be ascribed to SnS<sub>2</sub>. These structural features are consistent with those reported results obtained at similar hydrothermal reaction temperature conditions. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref34" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref34" aria-label="reference 34">(34)</a></div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c17466/asset/images/medium/am1c17466_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c17466/asset/images/large/am1c17466_0004.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. SEM images of (a) r-G/S, (b) 7% Co-SnS<sub>2</sub>@r-G, and (c) 7% Co-SnS<sub>2</sub>/S@r-G; TEM images of (d) r-G/S, (e) 7% Co-SnS<sub>2</sub>@r-G, and (f) 7% Co-SnS<sub>2</sub>/S@r-G; HRTEM images of (g) 7% Co-SnS<sub>2</sub>@r-G and (h) 7% Co-SnS<sub>2</sub>/S@r-G; energy-dispersive X-ray (EDX) elemental mapping of (i) r-G/S and (j) 7% Co-SnS<sub>2</sub>/S@r-G.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c17466/asset/images/large/am1c17466_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.1c17466&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c17466/asset/images/medium/am1c17466_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c17466/asset/images/large/am1c17466_0005.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. Cycling performance of the electrodes at a rate of 200 mA g<sup>–1</sup> (a), 1 A g<sup>–1</sup> (b), and 2 A g<sup>–1</sup> (c). (d) Rate performances of the electrodes; (e) long-term cycling performance of 7% Co-SnS<sub>2</sub>/S@r-G electrodes under different current densities (<i>I</i> represents the current density).</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c17466/asset/images/large/am1c17466_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.1c17466&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p last">The morphology of the samples was further observed by TEM in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 3</a>d–f and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c17466/suppl_file/am1c17466_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S7</a>. As shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c17466/suppl_file/am1c17466_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7a</a>, the SnS<sub>2</sub> sample shows a solid aggregation of small nanoparticles. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>d, the r-G/S sample is composed of a certain thickness lamellae structure anchored in graphene sheets, which can be ascribed to the amorphous sulfur layer. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref18" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref18" aria-label="reference 18">(18)</a> For <i>x</i>% Co-SnS<sub>2</sub>/S@r-G samples in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c17466/suppl_file/am1c17466_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7c–f</a>, all samples are composed of thick SnS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets with some long lamellae single S-like r-G/S samples. As shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c17466/suppl_file/am1c17466_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7g</a>, the lamellae structure of the sample becomes less with the volatilization of sulfur after calcination at 400 °C, leaving large numbers of flake composite structures. For 7% Co-SnS<sub>2</sub>@r-G and 7% Co-SnS<sub>2</sub>@r-G(150) shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c17466/suppl_file/am1c17466_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7h,i</a>, almost no lamellar structure as shown in the above figure is visible, leaving only a uniform two-dimensional sheet structure. HRTEM (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 3</a>g,h and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c17466/suppl_file/am1c17466_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S8</a>) was used to further study the microstructure of the samples. In <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c17466/suppl_file/am1c17466_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8a</a>, the typical lattice distances of 2.68 and 1.80 Å are ascribed to the (101) and (110) facets of SnS<sub>2</sub>, respectively. For <i>x</i>% Co-SnS<sub>2</sub>/S@r-G samples, all of them are composed of a certain thickness of layered SnS<sub>2</sub> with an obvious lattice and some shadow areas composed of an amorphous S layer. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref35" aria-label="reference 35">(35)</a> However, for the 7% Co-SnS<sub>2</sub>@r-G sample, there is only a layered SnS<sub>2</sub> structure. In addition, it can be seen that the lattice spacing of Co-doped SnS<sub>2</sub> is larger than that of undoped SnS<sub>2</sub> samples, which is conducive to the entrance and exit of Na<sup>+</sup> ions during cycling. All of these analyses are consistent with the above XRD analysis. The EDX mapping (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>i) shows that S is successfully composited into a composite and doped into graphene with a homogeneous distribution in the r-G/S sample. From EDX mapping of 7% Co-SnS<sub>2</sub>/S@r-G shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>j, it can be concluded that Co has been successfully doped into the SnS<sub>2</sub> structure with a homogeneous distribution.</div></div><div id="sec3_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i9" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i9">3.2.  Characterization of Electrochemical Performance</h3><div class="NLM_p">The electrochemical performances of the as-prepared electrodes under different current densities are given in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 4</a> and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c17466/suppl_file/am1c17466_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S9</a>. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>a,b, r-G shows relatively high initial discharge capacities of 543 and 497 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> but only retains very low reversible capacities of 20 and 15 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> under 0.2 and 1 A g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. Due to the absence of the doping element (e.g., S, N, or P) in the carbon structure, r-G alone contributes almost negligible sodium storage capacity. Compared with r-G, the r-G/S composite shows high initial discharge capacities of 749 and 651 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> and maintains the stable reversible capacities of 160 and 115 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.2 and 1 A g<sup>–1</sup> over 100 cycles, respectively. The increase of the specific capacity for r-G/S can be attributed to the composition and/or doping of S in the r-G. For SnS<sub>2</sub>, although it has high first discharge capacities of 1014 and 697 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> due to the conversion and alloying reactions, the serious attenuation leads to low discharge capacities of only 290 and 50 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.2 and 1 A g<sup>–1</sup> after 100 cycles, respectively, because of its low conductivity and a large volume effect of the granular structure in SnS<sub>2</sub>. Due to the uniform two-dimensional structure and sulfur composite, the SnS<sub>2</sub>/S@r-G electrode shows the high first discharge specific capacities of 800 and 746 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> and retains super stable reversible capacities of 606 and 590 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.2 and 1 A g<sup>–1</sup> over 100 cycles, respectively. Furthermore, the cycling performance and sodium storage capacity of the composite electrode can be continued to improve with Co doping. As shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c17466/suppl_file/am1c17466_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S9a,b</a>, the 3% Co-SnS<sub>2</sub>/S@r-G electrode shows high initial discharge capacities of 970 and 797 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> and holds high reversible capacities of 709 and 645 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> under 0.2 and 1 A g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. It is noteworthy that the 7% Co-SnS<sub>2</sub>/S@r-G electrode displays higher initial discharge capacities of 1148 and 1044 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> with initial Coulombic efficiencies (CE) of 70.3 and 75.5% and holds the highest reversible capacities of 878 and 791 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> under 0.2 and 1 A g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. However, the reversible sodium storage capacity begins to decline with the increase of Co doping. The 9% Co-SnS<sub>2</sub>/S@r-G and 11% Co-SnS<sub>2</sub>/S@r-G electrodes can hold reversible capacities of 839/728 and 687/680 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> over 100 cycles under 0.2 and 1 A g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. Compared with 7% Co-SnS<sub>2</sub>@r-G, the 7% Co-SnS<sub>2</sub>/S@r-G(400) electrode shows the initial discharge capacities of 786 and 635 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> and retains lower reversible capacities of 547 and 480 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> under 0.2 and 1 A g<sup>–1</sup> over 100 cycles, respectively. For 7% Co-SnS<sub>2</sub>@r-G(150), 7% Co-SnS<sub>2</sub>@r-G(180), and 7% Co-SnS<sub>2</sub>@r-G, all of them show relatively low reversible specific capacities (about 445 and 390 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> under 0.2 and 1 A g<sup>–1</sup> over 100 cycles, respectively) and fast decay rates (0.08–0.10% per one cycle compared with a negligible capacity loss of 7% Co-SnS<sub>2</sub>/S@r-G). The 7% Co-SnS<sub>2</sub>/S@r-G(100) electrode delivers stable but slightly low reversible capacities of 591 and 501 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> under 0.2 and 1 A g<sup>–1</sup> over 100 cycles, respectively. As shown as in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 4</a>c and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c17466/suppl_file/am1c17466_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S9c</a>, for a higher current of 2 A g<sup>–1</sup>, the 7% Co-SnS<sub>2</sub>/S@r-G electrode still has the best electrochemical performance than others, showing high first charge/discharge capacities of 740/936 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> with the first CE of 79.1% and retaining the highest reversible specific capacities of 772/774 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 2 A g<sup>–1</sup> over 100 cycles. The relatively high first CE in the as-prepared electrode can be mainly attributed to the design of two-dimensional composite electrode materials with high conductivity. Sodium ions are easy to enter and exit from the complex structure and active substances. Meanwhile, we also calculated pure SnS<sub>2</sub> contribution in composites and displayed it in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c17466/suppl_file/am1c17466_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S9d</a>. It can be found that SnS<sub>2</sub> in the 7% Co-SnS<sub>2</sub>/S@r-G electrode can provide almost 100% capacity contribution based on the theoretical specific capacity of sodium storage.</div><div class="NLM_p">The rate capabilities of SnS<sub>2</sub>/S@r-G, 7% Co-SnS<sub>2</sub>/S@r-G, and 7% Co-SnS<sub>2</sub>@r-G electrodes are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>d. Among them, the 7% Co-SnS<sub>2</sub>/S@r-G electrode presents more remarkable rate performances of about 860, 820, 763, 727, 666, 625, 570, 501, 433, 359, 296, and 250 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at various current densities of 0.2, 1, 2, 3, 5, 7, 10, 15, 20, 30, 40, and 50 A g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. When the current density returns to 0.2 A g<sup>–1</sup>, the reversible specific capacity suddenly increases to 877 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, indicating that the structure of 7% Co-SnS<sub>2</sub>/S@r-G retains excellent robustness.</div><div class="NLM_p">The long-term cycling performance of 7% Co-SnS<sub>2</sub>/S@r-G is also tested at the current densities of 0.5, 5, and 10 A g<sup>–1</sup> for 1000 cycles. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>e, the Co-SnS<sub>2</sub>/S@r-G electrode delivers the initial charge/discharge capacities of 779/1065 with a first CE of 73.2% and maintains the capacities of 820/829 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.5 A g<sup>–1</sup> over 1000 cycles. The reversible capacity increases slightly and is stable with almost no attenuation before the 1000th cycle. Impressively, the electrode still displays the super long cycling performance under a high current density, which retains high reversible specific capacities of 603/608 and 469/470 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> under 5 and 10 A g<sup>–1</sup>over 1000 cycles, respectively. To the best of our knowledge, as shown in <a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl1" href="#tbl1">Table 1</a>, the excellent performance of long-term cyclability at such high rates is rarely reported for TMS-based anodes of SIBs. This characteristic is very significant for the development of high-power battery technology.</div><div class="NLM_table-wrap" id="tbl1"><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption"><div class="NLM_caption" id=""><div class="title2">Table 1.
