class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">433 (20&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>–1</sup>)</td></tr><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">296 (40&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>–1</sup>)</td></tr><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">250 (50&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>–1</sup>)</td></tr></tbody></table></div></div></div><div id="sec3_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i11" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i11">3.3.  Study on the Sodium Storage Mechanism</h3><div class="NLM_p">To explore the mechanism of electrochemical sodium storage, cyclic voltammetry (CV) tests were carried out at 0.01–3.00 V vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na. For the 7% Co-SnS<sub>2</sub>/S@r-G electrode in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>a, a small peak at 2.15 V during the first cathodic scan is ascribed to the reversible phase transformation from element sulfur to sodium polysulfide. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref50 ref51" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref50 ref51" aria-label="reference 50 and 51">(50,51)</a> However, for the following scans, the reduction peaks shift to 1.71 V. A slight slope change between 1.55 and 0.93 V is ascribed to the Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation into the SnS<sub>2</sub> layer and the irreversible formation of the solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) film. The smaller change range here indicates that the Na<sup>+</sup> has less damage to the structure. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref12" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref12" aria-label="reference 12">(12)</a> The reduction peak at 0.20 V can be ascribed to the conversion reaction from SnS<sub>2</sub> to metallic Sn and the following alloying reaction of Sn with Na<sup>+</sup>. Meanwhile, it should be noted that the reduction peaks of 0.20 V in the following scans shift to 0.52 and 0.61 V. Moreover, a small cathodic peak at 0.09 V represents the Na<sup>+</sup> insertion in the graphene matrix. In the anodic process, a small oxidation peak at 0.10 V can be ascribed to Na<sup>+</sup> desorption from the graphene matrix. A small oxidation peak at 0.32 V corresponds to the desodiation reaction process from Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Sn to Sn. A predominant and broad oxidative peak in the curve appeared at 1.23–1.26 V because of the conversion reaction from Sn to SnS<sub>2</sub>. Additionally, three higher voltage oxidation peaks (about 2.02, 2.20, and 2.61 V, respectively) can be found in the curve, which can be attributed to the multistep oxidation of sodium sulfide to sodium polysulfides. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref52" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref52" aria-label="reference 52">(52)</a><sup>,</sup> <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref53" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref53" aria-label="reference 53">(53)</a><a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c17466/suppl_file/am1c17466_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S10</a> can provide more evidence for the above inference. For the SnS<sub>2</sub>/S@r-G electrode, the first cathodic scan curve is almost the same as that with the 7% Co-SnS<sub>2</sub>/S@r-G electrode in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>a. In the first anodic process, there is only one small peak near 1.60 V due to the formation of SnS<sub>2</sub>. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref46" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref46" aria-label="reference 46">(46)</a> There are also two small peaks in high voltage accessories at 2.03 and 2.20 V in the anodic process and one peak at 1.95 V in the cathode process, which stand for sulfur oxidation and reduction reactions. The 7% Co-SnS<sub>2</sub>@r-G electrode has a large difference in the first cathodic scan process, such as the obvious reduction peak at 1.69 V due to the strong insertion of Na<sup>+</sup> into SnS<sub>2</sub> and the formation of the SEI film. Furthermore, there are almost no redox peaks around the high potential, which are the major difference in the CV curve from that in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c17466/suppl_file/am1c17466_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S10</a>. Besides, the obvious redox peak appears near the high potential on the r-G/S curve. This is similar to <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>a and also proves that it is the redox reaction of sulfur. Based on the above pieces of evidence and the literature reported, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref8 ref11 ref47 ref54" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref8 ref11 ref47 ref54" aria-label="reference 8, 11, 47 and 54">(8,11,47,54)</a> the overall reaction processes of the 7% Co-SnS<sub>2</sub>/S@r-G electrode can be described as <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq1" aria-label="eqs 1">eqs 1</a>–<a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq5" aria-label="5">5</a><span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq1"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c17466/asset/images/medium/am1c17466_m001.gif" alt="" id="_i12"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(1)</span></div></div></span><span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq2"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c17466/asset/images/medium/am1c17466_m002.gif" alt="" id="_i13"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(2)</span></div></div></span><span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq3"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c17466/asset/images/medium/am1c17466_m003.gif" alt="" id="_i14"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(3)</span></div></div></span><span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq4"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c17466/asset/images/medium/am1c17466_m004.gif" alt="" id="_i15"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(4)</span></div></div></span><span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq5"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c17466/asset/images/medium/am1c17466_m005.gif" alt="" id="_i16"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(5)</span></div></div></span>Furthermore, compared with the CV curves of different electrodes in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>b, the 7% Co-SnS<sub>2</sub>/S@r-G electrode shows higher peak current and better coincidence, suggesting its higher sodiation/desodiation capacities and excellent reversibility.</div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c17466/asset/images/medium/am1c17466_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c17466/asset/images/large/am1c17466_0006.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. (a) CV profiles of the 7% Co-SnS<sub>2</sub>/S@r-G electrode at 0.2 mV s<sup>–1</sup>; (b) 2nd CV profiles of the as-prepared electrodes at 0.2 mV s<sup>–1</sup>; (c) capacitive and diffusion contributions of 7% Co-SnS<sub>2</sub>/S@r-G at 0.4 and 1.0 mV s<sup>–1</sup>; (d) normalized contribution ratio of capacitive capacities at different scan rates; (e) Nyquist plots for the electrodes after 10 cycles; the spots correspond to the experimental data and the solid lines stand for the calculated data. Inset: equivalent circuit diagram; (f) Nyquist plots for the 7% Co-SnS<sub>2</sub>/S@r-G electrode after different cycles under 0.2 A g<sup>–1</sup>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c17466/asset/images/large/am1c17466_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.1c17466&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">To further confirm the electrochemical reaction mechanism of 7% Co-SnS<sub>2</sub>/S@r-G electrode, ex situ XPS and TEM are employed to characterize the chemical composition and structure change on the electrode surface in the first charge/discharge process. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>a displays the Sn 3d XPS characterization of the electrode after the first charge/discharge process. Compared with the original Co-SnS<sub>2</sub>/S@r-G (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c17466/suppl_file/am1c17466_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2e</a>), the position and shape of the Sn 3d XPS peak of the electrode change greatly after cycling due to the Na<sup>+</sup> de/intercalation, especially the Auger effect of sodium (497.3 eV). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref55" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref55" aria-label="reference 55">(55)</a> On the fully discharged state (0.01 V), there are six Sn peaks of Sn<sup>4+</sup> 3d<sub>5/2</sub> and 3d<sub>3/2</sub> at 486.9/495.6 eV, Sn<sup>2+</sup> 3d<sub>5/2</sub> and 3d<sub>3/2</sub> at 486.2/494.5 eV, and Sn<sup>0</sup> 3d<sub>5/2</sub> and 3d<sub>3/2</sub> at 485.3/493.9 eV. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref56 ref57" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref56 ref57" aria-label="reference 56 and 57">(56,57)</a> The high intensity of Sn<sup>4+</sup> and Sn<sup>2+</sup> peaks may be ascribed to the high surface collection density from the surface oxidation of high active Sn<sup>0</sup> during electrode plate treatment after cycling. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref58" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref58" aria-label="reference 58">(58)</a> On a fully charged state (2.9 V), the Sn<sup>0</sup> 3d peaks disappeared, but the two strong peaks of Sn<sup>4+</sup> 3d return (8.4 eV of the separation distance between Sn 3d<sub>5/2</sub> and Sn 3d<sub>3/2</sub>), indicating a high reversible reaction (Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Sn is converted to SnS<sub>2</sub>). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref10" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref10" aria-label="reference 10">(10)</a> For the S 2p peak in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>b, it can also be found that the position and shape of the main XPS peaks changed greatly after cycling. The binding energies of S 2p peaks ascribing to S<sup>2–</sup> for the discharge and charge samples are 161.4 and 162.3 eV, respectively. The 0.9 eV energy level difference indicated the electrochemical oxidation of S during the charge process. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref59" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref59" aria-label="reference 59">(59)</a> When the cell is discharged to 0.01 V, the peaks at 164.1 eV representing the S–S bond and at 164.8 eV representing the S–C bond (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>b) are disappeared, mainly replaced by the peaks of Na<sub>2</sub>S<sub>2</sub> (160.7 eV)/Na<sub>2</sub>S (163.4 eV). In the fully charged state (2.9 V), it is obvious that the main peaks from elemental S (163.9 eV) appear like the initial product along with the peaks of Na<sub>2</sub>S<sub>2</sub>/Na<sub>2</sub>S vanished, indicating that a steady reversible reaction takes place in elemental sulfur. S 2p peaks between 166 and 170 eV corresponding to −SO<sub>3</sub>Na and thiosulfate/sulfate can be found in the fully discharged state, but the −SO<sub>3</sub>Na disappears at the fully charged state. These phenomena are very similar to the reaction mechanism of sulfur in the Na–S battery. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref51 ref60" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref51 ref60" aria-label="reference 51 and 60">(51,60)</a> The above XPS analysis again shows that S participates in the electrode reaction as an active material as same as SnS<sub>2</sub>, thus contributing to the sodium storage capacity. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>c–f shows the ex situ HRTEM images for the Co-SnS<sub>2</sub>/S@r-G electrode after first cycling. Both of the characteristic <i>d</i>-spaces of 7.41 and 2.68 Å correspond to the Na–Sn alloy (Na<sub>9</sub>Sn<sub>4</sub>, PDF: 31-1326) at the fully discharged state (0.01 V). The <i>d</i>-spaces of 3.20 and 2.21 Å are ascribed to the SnS<sub>2</sub> at the fully charged state (2.9 V). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref10" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref10" aria-label="reference 10">(10)</a> These results further support the inference of the reaction mechanism of CV analysis. According to the mapping map in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 8</a>c–f, it can be clearly seen that sodium ions are stored in the active material through alloying or adsorption during the discharge process, and almost all of them are separated from the active material during the charging process. At the same time, the material structure can basically maintain under the quasi reversible behavior of the electrochemical reaction.</div><figure id="fig6" data-index="6" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 6</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c17466/asset/images/medium/am1c17466_0007.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c17466/asset/images/large/am1c17466_0007.jpeg" id="gr6" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 6. (a) Ex situ Sn 3d and (b) S 2p XPS of the Co-SnS<sub>2</sub>/S@r-G electrode after the first discharge/charge process; (c, d) Ex situ HRTEM images for the Co-SnS<sub>2</sub>/S@r-G electrode after the first discharge and (e, f) charge process.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c17466/asset/images/large/am1c17466_0007.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.1c17466&amp;id=fig6"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p"><a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c17466/suppl_file/am1c17466_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S11a</a> displays the CV curves of the 7% Co-SnS<sub>2</sub>/S@r-G electrode at different scan rates. It can be seen that four peak current positions are basically not shifted, and the peak current increases in the vertical direction with the increasing scan rate. This indicates an extremely fast reaction and a small polarization of the composite material. <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c17466/suppl_file/am1c17466_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S11b</a> shows the CV curves of the SnS<sub>2</sub>/S@r-G electrode at different scan rates. It can be seen that the two peak current positions of peak 1 and peak 2 are slightly shifted, but the two peak current positions of peak 3 and peak 4 are obviously tilted. This indicated that the reversibility of the electrode reaction begins to become poor, especially for peak 3/peak 4 pair on the SnS<sub>2</sub>/S@r-G electrode. To estimate the effect of pseudocapacitance on the electrode reaction, a plot of log(<i>i</i>) vs log(<i>v</i>) is obtained on the relationship between the peak current (<i>i</i>) and the scan rate (<i>v</i>): <i>i</i> = <i>av<sup>b</sup></i> (<i>a</i> and <i>b</i> are constants). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref61" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref61" aria-label="reference 61">(61)</a> As shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c17466/suppl_file/am1c17466_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S11c,d</a>, the slopes for anodic peaks are between 0.5 and 1.0, implying that they are almost controlled by the surface-controlled charge storage or capacitive reaction for all three electrodes. Furthermore, there is a higher <i>b</i> value for the 7% Co-SnS<sub>2</sub>/S@r-G electrode (0.99 vs 0.91 and 0.84). For a battery material, when the <i>b</i> value is close to 1, it exactly means that the redox kinetics is as fast as a pseudocapacitive material. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref62" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref62" aria-label="reference 62">(62)</a> By separating the current response <i>i</i> at a fixed potential scan rate <i>v</i> into a capacitive contribution (<i>k</i><sub>1</sub><i>v</i>) and a diffusion-controlled process (<i>k</i><sub>2</sub><i>v</i><sup>1/2</sup>), a quantitative analysis of the contribution of the Na<sup>+</sup> capacitive effect can be conducted. In <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>c, when the scan rate is increased, the calculated capacitive contribution is increased, while the diffusion contribution is suppressed. It can be concluded that the capacities of these electrodes at a high potential rate are mainly contributed by “pseudocapacitance”. For the 7% Co-SnS<sub>2</sub>/S@r-G electrode, especially at a potential scan rate of 1.0 mV s<sup>–1</sup>, the calculated capacitive contribution almost covers the whole area (92.7%), indicating that the “pseudocapacitive” Na<sup>+</sup> storage contributes most of the capacity. This indicates that the surface-dominated extrinsic pseudocapacitance is identified as a major energy-storage mechanism.</div><div class="NLM_p">To further investigate the mechanism for the improved Na<sup>+</sup> storage, electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) measurements were also conducted. The Nyquist plots obtained from EIS of the electrodes after 10 cycles at 500 mA g<sup>–1</sup> are given in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 5</a>e and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c17466/suppl_file/am1c17466_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S12</a>. All of the Nyquist plots are composed of a depressed semicircle at high and intermediate frequencies and a sloped line at low frequency. The inset of <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>e shows the equivalent circuit where <i>R</i><sub>c</sub> represents the electrolyte and electrode resistance, <i>R</i><sub>sf</sub> represents the Na<sup>+</sup> migration resistance through the SEI film, <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> is the charge-transfer resistance, CPE<sub>1</sub> represents the passivation film capacitance, CPE<sub>2</sub> represents the electric double-layer capacitance, and <i>W</i> represents the semi-infinite Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion into the bulk electrode. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref25 ref63 ref64" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref25 ref63 ref64" aria-label="reference 25, 63 and 64">(25,63,64)</a> After fitting the EIS results by the proposed model, the values of all components are obtained, and the results are given in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c17466/suppl_file/am1c17466_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S2</a>. The <i>R</i><sub>c</sub> values of 7% Co-SnS<sub>2</sub>/S@r-G and 9% Co-SnS<sub>2</sub>/S@r-G electrodes are smaller than other electrodes. This shows that the electronic conductivity can be greatly improved after modifying with conductive graphene as well as the suitable Co doping in SnS<sub>2</sub>. The lower <i>R</i><sub>sf</sub> and <i>W</i> values for the 7% Co-SnS<sub>2</sub>/S@r-G electrode than others represent smaller barriers through the SEI film and active substance as well as quicker diffusion process of Na<sup>+</sup>. The increased <i>Y</i> and reduced <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> values for the 7% Co-SnS<sub>2</sub>/S@r-G electrode compared to others represent the increased electrical double-layer capacitance, which is beneficial to the charge-transfer reaction. Especially, the 7% Co-SnS<sub>2</sub>/S@r-G electrode owns the least charge-transfer resistance (∼1/11 and ∼1/6 time) and <i>W</i> value (∼1/22 and ∼1/4 time), and the highest electric double-layer capacitance (∼10 000 and ∼1600 time) than that of SnS<sub>2</sub> and 7% Co-SnS<sub>2</sub>@r-G electrodes, respectively. All of these results demonstrate that the electron transfer and Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion are faster for the 7% Co-SnS<sub>2</sub>/S@r-G electrode, which will be greatly beneficial to enhancing the reaction rate and cycling performance, especially for the electrochemical reaction process with deep galvanostatic cycles. EIS spectra after different cycles demonstrate the low and steady resistance values of the 7% Co-SnS<sub>2</sub>/S@r-G electrode from the 10th cycle onward (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>f), suggesting the stable charge-transfer behaviors on the electrode/electrolyte interface. At the same time, from the SEM and HRTEM images of the electrode surface after 50 and 200 cycles (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c17466/suppl_file/am1c17466_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figures S13 and S14</a>), it can be seen that the electrode remains intact after many cycles, and the nanosheet morphology of the microstructure is still maintained. The results demonstrate that the layered composite structure is beneficial for relieving the volume variation of the active substance during the discharge/charge process, therefore reinforcing the structural stability of the 7% Co-SnS<sub>2</sub>/S@r-G electrode in the repeated cycles.</div><div class="NLM_p">Based on the first principle method, the mechanism of Co doping and its effect on sodium storage performance were further analyzed. The theoretical calculation based on the Perdew–Burke–Ernzerhof algorithm and van der Waals correction DFT-D2 was carried out to simulate the adsorption and diffusion behaviors of Na<sup>+</sup> (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c17466/suppl_file/am1c17466_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S15</a>). The top site doping models for Na<sup>+</sup> adsorption were used to determine the optimized geometry configurations based on a single SnS<sub>2</sub> and one Co-doping site (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c17466/suppl_file/am1c17466_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S16</a>). From <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c17466/suppl_file/am1c17466_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S3</a>, it can be found that the proposed D1 site for Na<sup>+</sup> adsorption configuration is energetically more stable because its adsorption energy (−2.71 eV) is lower than D2 (0.011 eV), D3 (0.043 eV), D4 (0.046 eV), and D5 (0.057 eV). Then, the D1 configuration is chosen to explore the effect of Co doping on the diffusion barrier of Na<sup>+</sup> through undoped and Co-doped SnS<sub>2</sub>, and the diffusion routes for Na<sup>+</sup> transfer are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 7</a>a. The result shows that the adsorption energy of the undoped SnS<sub>2</sub> is −1.29 eV, while that of Co-doped SnS<sub>2</sub> is −2.71 eV, indicating that cobalt doping can greatly improve the adsorption capacity of sodium ions. At the same time, the transition state of undoped and Co-doped SnS<sub>2</sub> increases from 0.16 to 0.17 eV, showing an ignored effect of Co doping on the diffusion and mobility of Na<sup>+</sup>. Finally, the deformation charge density map and the density of states for the undoped and Co-doped SnS<sub>2</sub> are presented in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 7</a>b,c. Obviously, the charge accumulation near the Co-doped site shows an improvement of electronegativity and electrochemical activity, which is helpful to obtain excellent capacitance performance. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref65 ref66" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref65 ref66" aria-label="reference 65 and 66">(65,66)</a> Furthermore, from the density of states images, the band gap of Co-doped SnS<sub>2</sub> materials is smaller than that of SnS<sub>2</sub>, which is due to the formation of defects. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref67" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref67" aria-label="reference 67">(67)</a> The results show that the electric conductivity after being doped with Co for SnS<sub>2</sub> has been greatly improved, which further indicates that Co-doped SnS<sub>2</sub> has excellent rate performance.</div><figure id="fig7" data-index="7" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 7</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c17466/asset/images/medium/am1c17466_0008.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c17466/asset/images/large/am1c17466_0008.jpeg" id="gr7" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 7. (a) DFT calculation of the adsorption energy and corresponding potential diffusion barrier profiles of Na onto undoped and Co-doped SnS<sub>2</sub>. The Fermi level is set to zero at the dashed red line. (b) Deformation charge density map of Co-doped SnS<sub>2</sub>. (c) Density of states of undoped and Co-doped SnS<sub>2</sub>. (d) Schematic illustration of high-rate charge storage of Co-SnS<sub>2</sub>/S@graphene composite architecture as the SIB anode electrode.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c17466/asset/images/large/am1c17466_0008.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.1c17466&amp;id=fig7"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p last">The results presented above demonstrate sodium-ion storage with both high capacity and high rate capability rendered by extrinsic pseudocapacitance, low impedance, high electronic conductivity, and a strong ability to adsorb sodium ions. All of these can be attributed to the special Co-doped SnS<sub>2</sub>/S@graphene composite structure (the schematic diagram is shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 7</a>d). First, the two-dimensional SnS<sub>2</sub> attaching to the graphene nanosheet structure with many exposed surfaces is beneficial to the rapid conduction of electrons; second, the three-dimensional SnS<sub>2</sub>/S@graphene composite structure broadens the interlayer space and enlarges the connection area between the electrolyte and the active substrate, helping reduce the diffusion barrier of Na<sup>+</sup> and enhance kinetics. During the transfer of ions and electrons, the solvated Na<sup>+</sup>/electron can easily enter into the open spaces between neighboring nanosheets on both the outer and inner surfaces. After desolvation of Na<sup>+</sup> on the surface of the layered composite architecture, rapid sodiation occurs by the surface-dominated extrinsic pseudocapacitance; third, the homogeneous Co doping on the SnS<sub>2</sub> matrix can effectively expand the lattice spacing and improve the electronic conductivity; fourth, the presence of sulfur in the complex helps in providing more active sites, especially at high potential. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref51" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref51" aria-label="reference 51">(51)</a> All of these factors eventually lead to the formation of a capacity process or a fast redox reaction process with no diffusion limitation on the 7% Co-SnS<sub>2</sub>/S@r-G electrode. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref68" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref68" aria-label="reference 68">(68)</a></div></div><div id="sec3_4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i17" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i17">3.4.  Study on the Full-Cell Performance</h3><div class="NLM_p">To further confirm 7% Co-SnS<sub>2</sub>/S@r-G as the anode material for practical SIB applications, full batteries were constructed using Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>@MWCNTs as the cathodes for SIBs. The experimental details are given in the <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c17466/suppl_file/am1c17466_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>. The synthesized Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>@MWCNTs shows a 3–10 μm porous lamellar with a rhombohedral Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> phase structure (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c17466/suppl_file/am1c17466_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figures S17 and S18</a>). The Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>@MWCNTs cathode shows a superior long cycling life for 400 cycles under 1C (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c17466/suppl_file/am1c17466_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S19</a>). <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 8</a>a displays a schematic illustration of the 7% Co-SnS<sub>2</sub>/S@r-G//Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>@MWCNTs full SIBs. The working potential window of the full SIBs is determined by the CV test results of each electrode in half-cell configuration (top of <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 8</a>b), which is set between 0.5 and 3.7 V (bottom of <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c17466/suppl_file/am1c17466_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure 11b</a>). During the charge process, Na<sup>+</sup> is extracted from the Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>@MWCNTs cathode and inserted into the 7% Co-SnS<sub>2</sub>/S@r-G anode. The Na<sup>+</sup> flow process is reversed during the discharging process. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref69 ref70" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref69 ref70" aria-label="reference 69 and 70">(69,70)</a></div><figure id="fig8" data-index="8" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 8</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c17466/asset/images/medium/am1c17466_0009.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c17466/asset/images/large/am1c17466_0009.jpeg" id="gr8" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 8. (a) Schematic illustration of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>//7% Co-SnS<sub>2</sub>/S@r-G full-cell. (b) CV curves of 7% Co-SnS<sub>2</sub>/S@r-G and Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub><i>/</i>MWCNTs in half-cells (top) and Na-ion full battery (bottom) at 0.2 mV s<sup>–1</sup>, indicating the voltage window of full-cell from 0.5 to 3.7 V. (c) Rate performance of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>/MWCNTs//7% Co-SnS<sub>2</sub>/S@r-G full-cell and (d) long-term cycling performance under 0.5 A g<sup>–1</sup>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c17466/asset/images/large/am1c17466_0009.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.1c17466&amp;id=fig8"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p last">The <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 8</a>c inset displayed the charge/discharge curves at different rates of 7% Co-SnS<sub>2</sub>/S@r-G//Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>@MWCNTs full SIBs in the voltage range of 0.5–3.7 V. With increasing rates, the voltage platform and reversible capacity decrease slightly. This proves to be a better rate performance for the Na-ion full-cell. The rate performance of the full-cell was tested, and the result is shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 8</a>c. The assembled full-cell exhibits a specific capacity of 562, 518, 481, 432, 381, and 328 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.2, 0.5, 1, 2, 3, and 5 A g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. Furthermore, when the current density goes back to 0.2 A g<sup>–1</sup>, the reversible capacity quickly recovers to 560 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>. This once again indicates that the stable structure of full-cell anode/cathode materials can withstand different current densities. More encouragingly, the full-cell also exhibits an outstanding long-term cycling performance under 0.5 A g<sup>–1</sup>, which delivers a reversible capacity of 500 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> over 200 cycles with a capacity retention of 97.3%, and still retains a high reversible capacity of 432 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> over 400 cycles with a capacity retention of 84.0% (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 8</a>d). The superior electrochemical performance of the sodium-ion full-cell demonstrates that the 7% Co-SnS<sub>2</sub>/S@r-G has competitive merits and great prospects for practical applications.</div></div></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i18">4.  Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">In this work, both S-compositing and Co-doping strategies for SnS<sub>2</sub>/graphene anode materials of SIBs are carried out by a one-step hydrothermal method under moderate conditions to improve sodium storage performance. The as-prepared SnS<sub>2</sub>/graphene composite (7% Co-SnS<sub>2</sub>/S@r-G) consisting of 15.4 wt % S and 1.49 atom % Co shows both superior cycling stability and rate capability to those reported in the literature. For example, the super stable reversible capacities of 829 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> (0.5 A g<sup>–1</sup>), 608 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> (5 A g<sup>–1</sup>), and 470 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at a current density of 10 A g<sup>–1</sup> over 1000 deep galvanostatic cycles are achieved. The theoretical calculation shows that Co doping can improve the adsorption energy of Na<sup>+</sup> and improve the conductivity without affecting the diffusion. The excellent half/full-cell performance can be attributed to the favorable three-dimensional structure of the composite, Co doping, and S-composition, which can induce a synergistic effect. The study provides a new strategy for designing TMS-based composite anodes for enhancing the performance of SIBs.</div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes-1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i19">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge at <a href="/doi/10.1021/acsami.1c17466?goto=supporting-info" class="ext-link">https://pubs.acs.org/doi/10.1021/acsami.1c17466</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">XRD patterns of the as-prepared samples; XPS spectra of survey spectra, the S/Sn atomic ratio, and Co atomic ratio, S 2p, C 1s, Sn 3d, and Co 2p for the samples; EDS test of the 7% Co-SnS<sub>2</sub>/S@r-G sample; Raman shift curves of full spectra; BET curve of the as-prepared samples; SEM, TEM, and HRTEM images of the as-prepared samples; cycling performance of the electrodes at a rate of 200 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, 1 A g<sup>–1</sup>, and 2 A g<sup>–1</sup>; CV profiles of the as-prepared electrodes under 0.2 mV s<sup>–1</sup>; CV curves of 7% Co-SnS<sub>2</sub>/S@r-G, and SnS<sub>2</sub>/S@r-G electrodes at different scan rates; relationship between the logarithm anodic peak current and logarithm scan rates; Nyquist plots for the electrodes; SEM and HRTEM of 7% Co-SnS<sub>2</sub>/S@r-G electrodes after 50 and 200 discharge/charge cycles; possible Na diffusion pathways and diffusion barrier profiles of the second layer on the SnS<sub>2</sub> and Co-doped SnS<sub>2</sub> system; various Na<sup>+</sup> adsorption configurations of Co-doped SnS<sub>2</sub> in their top views; XRD, SEM, and cycling performance of porous Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>@MWCNTs (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c17466/suppl_file/am1c17466_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c17466/suppl_file/am1c17466_si_001.pdf">am1c17466_si_001.pdf (3.13 MB)</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.1c17466" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget" style="display: block;"></div><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="85b54935-30e9-4058-a6d2-0b40fb754a85" class="mb-4">

                        cas_cit2R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">2</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">High-concentration ether-based electrolyte boosts the electrochemical performance of SnS2-reduced graphene oxide for K-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Xie, Junpeng; Zhu, Yongqian; Zhuang, Ning; Li, Xiaodan; Yuan, Xinran; Li, Jinliang; Hong, Guo; Mai, Wenjie</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry A: Materials for Energy and Sustainability</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2019</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">7</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">33</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">19332-19341</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JMCAET</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2050-7496</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">To meet the urgent demand for energy storage systems, K-ion batteries (KIBs), with low cost and comparable electrochem. performance, have become one of the most promising alternatives to Li-ion batteries.  In this study, nanocryst. SnS2 anchored to reduced graphene oxide (SnS2-RGO) is investigated using ether-based electrolytes.  A reversible specific capacity of 436 mA h g-1 at 100 mA g-1 after 50 cycles was obtained, as well as the reversible specific capacity of 311 mA h g-1 at 500 mA g-1 after 150 cycles, which are much better than those using ester-based electrolytes.  Interestingly, it was found that high-concn. ether-based electrolytes can remarkably suppress the growth of KxS needles, which is probably due to the electrostatic shielding effect induced by the high concn. of K ions.  This discovery should be important for K-ion storage and could shed light on the design of better KIBs from the perspective of electrolytes.  Furthermore, we also utilized ex situ XRD patterns to reveal the electrochem. reaction process and reaction intermediate products of SnS2-RGO with high-concn. ether-based electrolytes.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref3"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref3" class="refNumLink">3</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit3" data-doi="10.1007/s12598-020-01623-x"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, C.                         cas_cit3R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">3</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">SnO2 quantum dots modified N-doped carbon as high-performance anode for lithium ion batteries by enhanced pseudocapacitance</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wu, Cui-Ping; Xie, Kai-Xuan; He, Jia-Peng; Wang, Qing-Peng; Ma, Jian-Min; Yang, Shun; Wang, Qing-Hong</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Rare Metals (Beijing, China)</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2021</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">40</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">1</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">48-56</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">RARME8</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1001-0521</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Journal Publishing Center of University of Science and Technology Beijing</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">SnO2 is considered to be a promising candidate as anode material for lithium ion batteries, due to its high theor. specific capacity (1494 mAh.g-1).  Nevertheless, SnO2-based anodes suffer from poor electronic cond. and serious vol. variation (300%) during lithiation/delithiation process, leading to fast capacity fading.  To solve these problems, SnO2quantum dots modified N-doped carbon spheres (SnO2QDs@N-C) are fabricated by facile hydrolysis process of SnCl2, accompanied with the polymn. of polypyrrole (PPy), followed by a calcination method.  When used as anodes for lithium ion batteries, SnO2QDs@N-C exhibits high discharge capacity, superior rate properties as well as good cyclability.  The carbon matrix completely encapsulates the SnO2quantum dots, preventing the aggregation and vol. change during cycling.  Furthermore, the high N content produces abundant defects in carbon matrix.  It is worth noting that SnO2QDs@N-C shows excellent capacitive contribution properties, which may be due to the ultra-small size of SnO2and high cond. of the carbon matrix.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref4"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref4" class="refNumLink">4</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit4" data-doi="10.1002/adfm.201703390"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, Q.                         cas_cit6R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">6</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Two-dimensional nanostructures for sodium-ion battery anodes</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Mao, Jianfeng; Zhou, Tengfei; Zheng, Yang; Gao, Hong; Liu, Hua kun; Guo, Zaiping</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry A: Materials for Energy and Sustainability</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have attracted great attention recently due to the abundance of sodium resources, particularly for large-scale elec. energy storage applications for renewable energy and smart grids.  More and more nanostructured anode materials have been developed with the aims of high energy d., high cycling stability, and excellent rate capability, in which two-dimensional (2D) nanostructures are showing promise due to their shortened paths for sodium ion transportation and larger surface areas for sodium ion absorption.  Moreover, 2D materials (e.g. graphene) have been proved to be excellent supporting and conducting agents in SIB anodes due to their high elec. cond. and structural stability, in which synergetic effects between the graphene and the active materials are generally obsd.  This review is devoted to the recent progress in the use of 2D active materials and in composites consisting of both 2D supports and active materials as anodes for SIBs.  Based on the manner of sodium storage, their electrochem. performance for sodium storage is discussed in terms of four classifications, including carbonaceous materials (graphene and carbon nanosheets), alloy based materials (Sn, Sb, and P), conversion materials (phosphides/oxides/sulfides/selenides), and intercalation materials (Ti-based compds.).  Finally, the main challenges for and perspectives on 2D nanostructures for sodium storage are discussed.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref7"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref7" class="refNumLink">7</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit7" data-doi="10.1002/adma.201001068"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhu, Y.                         cas_cit8R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">8</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Scalable synthesis of SnS2/S-doped graphene composites for superior Li/Na-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zheng, Penglun; Dai, Zhengfei; Zhang, Yu; Dinh, Khang Ngoc; Zheng, Yun; Fan, Haosen; Yang, Jun; Dangol, Raksha; Li, Bing; Zong, Yun; Yan, Qingyu; Liu, Xiaobo</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nanoscale</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Tin disulfide (SnS2) has emerged as a promising anode material for lithium/sodium ion batteries (LIBs/SIBs) due to its unique layered structure, outstanding electrochem. properties and low cost.  However, its poor cycling life and time-consuming synthesis as well as low-yield prodn. hinder the practical utilization of nanostructured SnS2.  In this work, we demonstrate a simple and reliable dissoln.-regeneration strategy to construct a flexible SnS2/sulfur-doped reduced graphene oxide (S-rGO) composite as anodes for LIBs and SIBs, highlighting its mass-prodn. feature.  In addn., the robust affinity between SnS2 and S-rGO without interstitial vol. is very beneficial for preventing the SnS2 particles from breaking themselves away from the rGO nanosheets into free nanoparticles.  As a result, the SnS2/S-rGO composite as anodes delivers high reversible capacities of 1078 mA h g-1 and 564 mA h g-1 (at 0.1 A g-1) for LIBs and SIBs, resp., and excellent rate capabilities and cycling stability (e.g. 532 mA h g-1 during the 600 cycles at 5.0 A g-1 for LIBs).  Our proposed strategy may also possess great potential for the practical application of other electrochem. active metal sulfide composites for energy devices.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref9"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref9" class="refNumLink">9</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit9" data-doi="10.1039/C8CC04255A"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tao, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, D.         </div><div class="casAbstract">We report the effort in designing layered SnS2 nanocrystals decorated on nitrogen and sulfur dual-doped graphene aerogels (SnS2@N,S-GA) as anode material of SIBs.  The optimized mass loading of SnS2 along with the addn. of nitrogen and sulfur on the surface of GAs results in enhanced electrochem. performance of SnS2@N,S-GA composite.  In particular, the introduction of nitrogen and sulfur heteroatoms could provide more active sites and good accessibility for Na ions.  Moreover, the incorporation of the stable SnS2 crystal structure within the anode results in the superior discharge capacity of 527 mAh g-1 under a c.d. of 20 mA g-1 upon 50 cycles.  It maintains 340 mAh g-1 even the c.d. is increased to 800 mA g-1.  Aiming to further systematically study mechanism of composite with improved SIB performance, we construct the corresponding models based on exptl. data and conduct first-principles calcns.  The calcd. results indicate the sulfur atoms doped in GAs show a strong bridging effect with the SnS2 nanocrystals, contributing to build robust architecture for electrode.  Simultaneously, heteroatom dual doping of GAs shows the imperative function for improved elec. cond.  Herein, first-principles calcns. present a theor. explanation for outstanding cycling properties of SnS2@N,S-GA composite.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref12"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref12" class="refNumLink">12</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit12" data-doi="10.1002/cssc.201900719"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sun, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ci, L.</span></span><span class="NLM_etal"></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Surface-Confined SnS<sub>2</sub>@C@R<sub>g</sub>O as High-Performance Anode Materials for Sodium- and Potassium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>ChemSusChem</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2019</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>12</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">2689</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">2700</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Potassium- (PIBs) and sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are emerging as promising alternatives to lithium-ion batteries owing to the low cost and abundance of K and Na resources.  However, the large radius of K+ and Na+ lead to sluggish kinetics and relatively large vol. variations.  Herein, a surface-confined strategy is developed to restrain SnS2 in self-generated hierarchically porous carbon networks with an in situ reduced graphene oxide (rGO) shell (SnS2@C@rGO).  The as-prepd. SnS2@C@rGO electrode delivers high reversible capacity (721.9 mAh g-1 at 0.05 A g-1) and superior rate capability (397.4 mAh g-1 at 2.0 A g-1) as the anode material of SIB.  Furthermore, a reversible capacity of 499.4 mAh g-1 (0.05 A g-1) and a cycling stability with 298.1 mAh g-1 after 500 cycles at a c.d. of 0.5 A g-1 were achieved in PIBs, surpassing most of the reported non-carbonaceous anode materials.                         cas_cit16R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">16</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Interlayer expansion of few-layered Mo-doped SnS2 nanosheets grown on carbon cloth with excellent lithium storage performance for lithium ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Chen, Qiang; Lu, Fengqi; Xia, Ying; Wang, Hai; Kuang, Xiaojun</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry A: Materials for Energy and Sustainability</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">New conceptions of developing various nanostructure materials supported on conductive substrates are urgently required for additive-free integrated electrodes for lithium ion batteries (LIBs).  In this work, Mo-doped SnS2-based nanosheets were directly grown on a conductive substrate, carbon cloth, via a facile hydrothermal method.  Significantly, the as-synthesized samples possess a three-dimensional network structure consisting of interconnected nanosheets, which can be directly employed as additive-free integrated electrodes for LIBs.  The as-obtained CC@Sn0.9Mo0.1S2 nanosheets exhibit initial high discharge and charge capacities of 2033.6 and 1869.8 mA h g-1 at a c.d. of 1 A g-1, with a coulombic efficiency of 91.9%.  A high reversible discharge capacity of 1950.8 mA h g-1 is obtained after 200 cycles.  Moreover, a high reversible capacity of 914.5 mA h g-1 is achieved even at a high c.d. of 5 A g-1, which significantly exceeds the theor. capacity of com. graphite and reversible capacity of SnS2.  A full lithium ion battery was assembled composed of the CC@Sn0.9Mo0.1S2 anode and a com. LiCoO2 cathode, which also delivered high capacity and cycling stability.  The excellent electrochem. lithium storage performance of Mo-doped SnS2 could be attributed to the molybdenum doping, which limited the no. of layers of the nanosheets, enlarged the interlayer spacing and generated rich defects.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref17"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref17" class="refNumLink">17</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit17" data-doi="10.1038/s42004-018-0086-z"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jia, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Oladele, O.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tang, Y.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Mg-doped SnS2 nanoflowers were synthesized by hydrothermal method.  The XRD and absorption spectra analyses reveal that the incorporated Mg atoms substitute for Sn atoms in SnS2 lattice and red-shift the band-gap at low doping concn. (≤4 at%).  With further doping, a transformation of Mg atoms from substitutional sites to interstitial sites occurs.  The ferromagnetism of SnS2 nanoflowers is enhanced non-monotonously with Mg doping and the largest satn. magnetization of 2.11×10-3 emu/g appears in 4 at% Mg-doped SnS2.  The holes created by Mg substituted incorporation may be the origin of the ferromagnetism.  On the other hand, interstitial Mg atoms play a neg. role in enhancing the ferromagnetism due to the holes compensation effect.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref20"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref20" class="refNumLink">20</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit20" data-doi="10.1016/j.ijleo.2017.06.015"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Prabha, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ilangovan, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Balamurugan, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Suganya, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Anitha, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Nagarethinam, V. The S-rGO paper is synthesized through a facile freeze drying route followed by low-temp. heat treatment.  The flexible S-rGO paper not only provides a conductive framework for electron transport but also alleviates vol. effect during cycling.  The as-designed S-rGO paper exhibits excellent rate capability and cyclability.  The specific discharge capacity is 800 mAh g-1 after 200 cycles at a c.d. of 300 mA g-1 and the capacity fading rate is only 0.035% per cycle.                         cas_cit23R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">23</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Sulfur nanocrystals anchored graphene composite with highly improved electrochemical performance for lithium-sulfur batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zhang, Jun; Dong, Zimin; Wang, Xiuli; Zhao, Xuyang; Tu, Jiangping; Su, Qingmei; Du, Gaohui</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Power Sources</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2014</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">270</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">1-8</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JPSODZ</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0378-7753</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier B.V.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Two kinds of graphene-sulfur composites with 50 wt% of sulfur are prepd. using hydrothermal method and thermal mixing, resp.  Transmission Electron Microscopy (TEM) and Energy Dispersive X-ray Spectra mapping show that sulfur nanocrystals with size of ∼5 nm dispersed on graphene sheets homogeneously for the sample prepd. by hydrothermal method (NanoS@G).  While for the thermal mixed graphene-sulfur composite (S-G mixt.), sulfur shows larger and uneven size (50-200 nm).  X-ray Photoelectron Spectra (XPS) reveals the strong chem. bonding between the sulfur nanocrystals and graphene.  Comparing with the S-G mixt., the NanoS@G composite shows highly improved electrochem. performance as cathode for lithium-sulfur (Li-S) battery.  The NanoS@G composite delivers an initial capacity of 1400 mAh g-1 with the sulfur utilization of 83.7% at a c.d. of 335 mA g-1.  The capacity keeps above 720 mAh g-1 over 100 cycles.  The strong adherence of the sulfur nanocrystals on graphene immobilizes sulfur and polysulfides species and suppressed the "shuttle effect", resulting higher coulombic efficiency and better capacity retention.  Electrochem. impedance also suggests that the strong bonding enabled rapid electronic/ionic transport and improved electrochem. kinetics, therefore good rate capability is obtained.  These results demonstrate that the NanoS@G composite is a very promising candidate for high-performance Li-S batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref24"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref24" class="refNumLink">24</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit24" data-doi="10.1039/C6CS00776G"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hwang, J.                         cas_cit24R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">24</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Sodium-ion batteries: present and future</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Hwang, Jang-Yeon; Myung, Seung-Taek; Sun, Yang-Kook</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemical Society Reviews</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">46</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">12</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">3529-3614</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">CSRVBR</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0306-0012</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Energy prodn. and storage technologies have attracted a great deal of attention for day-to-day applications.  In recent decades, advances in lithium-ion battery (LIB) technol. have improved living conditions around the globe.  LIBs are used in most mobile electronic devices as well as in zero-emission electronic vehicles.  However, there are increasing concerns regarding load leveling of renewable energy sources and the smart grid as well as the sustainability of lithium sources due to their limited availability and consequent expected price increase.  Therefore, whether LIBs alone can satisfy the rising demand for small- and/or mid-to-large-format energy storage applications remains unclear.  To mitigate these issues, recent research has focused on alternative energy storage systems.  Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are considered as the best candidate power sources because sodium is widely available and exhibits similar chem. to that of LIBs; therefore, SIBs are promising next-generation alternatives.  Recently, sodiated layer transition metal oxides, phosphates and org. compds. have been introduced as cathode materials for SIBs.  Simultaneously, recent developments have been facilitated by the use of select carbonaceous materials, transition metal oxides (or sulfides), and intermetallic and org. compds. as anodes for SIBs.  Apart from electrode materials, suitable electrolytes, additives, and binders are equally important for the development of practical SIBs.  Despite developments in electrode materials and other components, there remain several challenges, including cell design and electrode balancing, in the application of sodium ion cells.  In this article, we summarize and discuss current research on materials and propose future directions for SIBs.  This will provide important insights into scientific and practical issues in the development of SIBs.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref25"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref25" class="refNumLink">25</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit25" data-doi="10.1016/j.electacta.2018.08.122"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, W. Herein, we report a cobalt metal cation doping strategy to enhance the electrochem. performance of a SnS2 nanosheet array anode through a facile hydrothermal method.  Benefitting from this special structure and heteroatom-doping effect, this anode material displays a high initial coulombic efficiency of 57.4%, a superior discharge specific capacity as high as 1288 mA h g-1 at 0.2 A g-1 after 100 cycles and outstanding long-term cycling stability with a reversible capacity of 800.4 mA h g-1 even at 2 A g-1. Besides, a carbon cloth substrate can maintain the integrity of the electrode material framework and buffer its vol. variation, thus boosting intrinsic dynamic properties and enhancing sodium storage performance.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref29"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref29" class="refNumLink">29</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit29" data-doi="10.1039/C7NR07427A"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Roy, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Joshi, A.         </div><div class="casAbstract">X-ray photoemission spectroscopy (XPS), X-ray diffraction (XRD) and transmission electron microscopy (TEM) have been used to study the structural and morphol. characteristics of cobalt doped tin(IV) oxide (Sn1-xCoxO2; 0 ≤ x ≤ 0.04) nanocrystals synthesized by a chem. co-pptn. technique.  Electronic structure anal. using X-ray photoemission spectroscopy (XPS) shows the formation of tin interstitials (Sni) and redn. of oxygen vacancies (VO) in the host lattice on Co doping and that the doped Co exists in mixed valence states of +2 and +3.  Using XRD, the preferential position of the Sni and doped Co in the unit cell of the nanocrystals have been estd.  Rietveld refinement of XRD data shows that samples are of single phase and variation of lattice consts. follows Vegard's law.  XRD and TEM measurements show that the crystallite size of the nanocrystals decrease with increase in Co doping concn.  SAED patterns confirm the monocryst. nature of the samples.  The study of the lattice dynamics using Raman spectroscopy and Fourier transform IR (FTIR) spectroscopy shows the existence of many disorder activated forbidden optical phonon modes, along with the corresponding classical modes, signifying Co induced local symmetry breaking in the nanocrystals.  UV-Vis spectroscopy shows that the optical band gap has red shifted with increase in doping concn.  The study of Urbach energy confirms the increase in disorder in the nanocrystals with Co doping.  Local symmetry breaking induced UV emission along with violet, blue and green luminescence has been obsd. from the PL study.  The spectral contribution of UV emission decreases and green luminescence increases with increase in doping.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A consistent method for calcn. of pore size distributions in nanoporous materials from adsorption and desorption isotherms is presented, which form the hysteresis loop H1 by the IUPAC classification.  The method is based on the nonlocal d. functional theory (NLDFT) of capillary condensation hysteresis in cylindrical pores.  It is implemented for the N2 and Ar sorption at their boiling temps.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A functionalized graphene sheet-sulfur (FGSS) nanocomposite was synthesized as the cathode material for lithium-sulfur batteries.  The structure has a layer of functionalized graphene sheets/stacks (FGS) and a layer of sulfur nanoparticles creating a three-dimensional sandwich-type architecture.  This unique FGSS nanoscale layered composite has a high loading (70 wt%) of active material (S), a high tap d. of ∼0.92 g cm-3, and a reversible capacity of ∼505 mAh g-1 (∼464 mAh cm-3) at a c.d. of 1680 mA g-1 (1C).         </div><div class="casAbstract">Na-ion batteries have been considered as promising alternatives to Li-ion batteries due to the natural abundance of sodium resources.  Searching for high-performance anode materials currently becomes a hot topic and also a great challenge for developing Na-ion batteries.  In this work, a novel hybrid anode is synthesized consisting of ultrafine, few-layered SnS2 anchored on few-layered reduced graphene oxide (rGO) by a facile solvothermal route.  The SnS2/rGO hybrid exhibits a high capacity, ultralong cycle life, and superior rate capability.  The hybrid can deliver a high charge capacity of 649 mA-h/g at 100 mA/g. The hybrid can also sustain a c.d. up to 12.8 A/g (≈28C) where the charge process can be completed in only 1.3 min while still delivering a charge capacity of 337 mA-h/g.                         cas_cit37R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">37</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Exfoliated-SnS2 restacked on graphene as a high-capacity, high-rate, and long-cycle life anode for sodium ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Liu, Yongchang; Kang, Hongyan; Jiao, Lifang; Chen, Chengcheng; Cao, Kangzhe; Wang, Yijing; Yuan, Huatang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nanoscale</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Designed as a high-capacity, high-rate, and long-cycle life anode for sodium ion batteries, exfoliated-SnS2 restacked on graphene is prepd. by the hydrolysis of lithiated SnS2 followed by a facile hydrothermal method.  Structural and morphol. characterizations demonstrate that ultrasmall SnS2 nanoplates (with a typical size of 20-50 nm) composed of 2-5 layers are homogeneously decorated on the surface of graphene, while the hybrid structure self-assembles into a three-dimensional (3D) network architecture.  The obtained SnS2/graphene nanocomposite delivers a remarkable capacity as high as 650 mA h g-1 at a c.d. of 200 mA g-1.  More impressively, the capacity can reach 326 mA h g-1 even at 4000 mA g-1 and remains stable at ∼610 mA h g-1 without fading up to 300 cycles when the rate is brought back to 200 mA g-1.  The excellent electrochem. performance is attributed to the synergetic effects between the ultrasmall SnS2 and the highly conductive graphene network.  The unique structure can simultaneously facilitate Na+ ion diffusion, provide more reaction sites, and suppress aggregation and vol. fluctuation of the active materials during prolonged cycling.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref38"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref38" class="refNumLink">38</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit38" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.5b02413"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chao, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Mao, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, C.</span></span><span class="NLM_etal"></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Solid-State Fabrication of SnS<sub>2</sub>/C Nanospheres for High-Performance Sodium Ion Battery Anode</span>. <i>ACS Appl.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Tin disulfide (SnS2) has emerged as a promising anode material for sodium ion batteries (NIBs) due to its unique layered structure, high theor. capacity, and low cost.  Conventional SnS2 nanomaterials are normally synthesized using hydrothermal method, which is time-consuming and difficult to scale up for mass prodn.  In this study, we develop a simple solid-state reaction method, in which the carbon-coated SnS2 (SnS2/C) anode materials were synthesized by annealing metallic Sn, sulfur powder, and polyacrylonitrile in a sealed vacuum glass tube.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Cost-effective sodium ion batteries (SIBs) are emerging as a desirable alternative choice to lithium ion batteries in terms of application in large-scale energy storage devices.  SnS2 is regarded as a potential anode material for SIBs because of its unique layered structure and high theor. specific capacity.  However, the development of SnS2 was hindered by the sluggish kinetics of the diffusion process and the inevitable vol. change during repeated sodiation- desodiation processes.  In this work, SnS2 with a unique nanowall array (NWA) structure is fabricated by one-step pulsed spray evapn. chem. vapor deposition (PSE-CVD), which could be used directly as binder-free and carbon-free anodes for SIBs.  The SnS2 NWA electrode achieves a high reversible capacity of 576 mAh g-1 at 500 mA g-1 and enhanced cycling stability.  Attractively, an excellent rate capability is demonstrated with ∼370 mAh g-1 at 5 A g-1, corresponding to a capacity retention of 64.2% at 500 mA g-1.                         cas_cit45R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">45</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Ultrahigh rate sodium-ion storage of SnS/SnS2 heterostructures anchored on S-doped reduced graphene oxide by ion-assisted growth</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Hu, Mingxiang; Zhang, Hongwei; Yang, Le; Lv, Ruitao</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Carbon</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Developing high-performance anode materials is very crucial for room-temp. sodium-ion batteries (SIBs).  Sn-based compds. are promising SIB anode materials for their high theor. capacities and low costs.  However, the intrinsic low cond., poor infiltration and irreversible reactions of Sn-based compds. lead to poor rate and cycling performance.  In order to address above issues, SnS/SnS2 heterostructures on sulfur-doped reduced graphene oxide (SG) are assembled via a facile alkali ion-assisted growth.  As-synthesized SnS/SnS2 heterostructures on SG with assistance of K+, denoted as SnS/SnS2@SG-K, exhibits typical layer-stacked structure and high reversible capacities over 800 mAh g-1 at 50 mA g-1 and 241 mAh g-1 even at a record high c.d. of 48 A g-1, which are superior to most of the Sn-based SIB anode materials.  The excellent rate and cycling performance of SnS/SnS2@SG-K could be ascribed to the layer-stacked structures of SG and heterojunctions between SnS and SnS2 which can accelerate semi-infinite diffusions of Na+ ions and electrons, alleviate polysulfide shuttling problems and reduce vol. fluctuation effect.         </div><div class="casAbstract">With the intensification of environmental pollution problems, the requirement for clean energy is increasing as time goes by.  Currently, lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) composed of graphite anode cannot meet the com. demand of human beings.                         cas_cit47R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">47</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Coherent SnS2/NiS2 hetero-nanosheet arrays with fast charge transfer for enhanced sodium-ion storage</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Guan, Shundong; Wang, Tianshi; Fu, Xiuli; Fan, Li-Zhen; Peng, Zhijian</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Applied Surface Science</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Layered SnS2 has attracted extensive attention as promising anode material for sodium-ion storage owing to its high theor. specific capacity. Herein, a novel coherent SnS2/NiS2 hetero-nanosheet arrays on carbon cloth (SnS2/NiS2@CC) is fabricated through a facile one-step solvothermal method by controlling the synchronous growth of SnS2 and NiS2.                         cas_cit48R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">48</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">SnS2 quantum dots uniformly anchored on dispersed S-doped graphene as high-rate anodes for sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Jin, Shibo; Sun, Xiaohong; Cai, Shu; Guo, Jinze; Fan, Anran; Zhang, Na; Wu, Han; Zheng, Chunming</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Ceramics International</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">SnS2 stands out to be a potential anode material for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) owing to its high theor. capacity and earth abundance.  Nevertheless, the severe vol. expansion and aggregation of SnS2 would lead to low reversible capacity and poor rate capability.  Herein, SnS2 quantum dots uniformly anchored on dispersed S-doped graphene were designed and synthesized via a simple hot bath process with the addn. of K+ (K-SnS2@SG).                         cas_cit52R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">52</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A Soft Approach to Encapsulate Sulfur: Polyaniline Nanotubes for Lithium-Sulfur Batteries with Long Cycle Life</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Xiao, Lifen; Cao, Yuliang; Xiao, Jie; Schwenzer, Birgit; Engelhard, Mark H.; Saraf, Laxmikant V.; Nie, Zimin; Exarhos, Gregory J.; Liu, Jun</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Materials (Weinheim, Germany)</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A novel SPANI-NT/S mol. composite was synthesized in nanotube form by in situ vulcanization through heating a mixt. of PANI-NT and sulfur at 280°.  The electrode retains a discharge capacity of 837 mA-h/g after 100 cycles at a 0.1 C rate.  Even at a discharge rate of 1 C, the electrode manifested very stable cycling capacity up to 500 cycles.  The factors that contribute to the reliable rate cycling obsd. in the SPANI-HWN/S composite are discussed.  The unique properties of the soft polymer plus their simple fabrication make this class of materials attractive for further investigation for Li-S batteries applications.  The properties of the soft polymer nanostructures are different from those of rigid carbon materials, and the combination of the complementary properties may further improve the stability of Li-S batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref53"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref53" class="refNumLink">53</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit53" data-doi="10.1149/1.1503076"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Shim, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Striebel, K. The variations of capacity and capacity fade rate were also measured as a function of the content of S, C and binder.  The capacity fade rate of the sulfur electrode was lower with a higher mol. wt. solvent such as PEGDME 250 or 500.                         cas_cit55R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">55</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Lithium-activated SnS-graphene alternating nanolayers enable dendrite-free cycling of thin sodium metal anodes in carbonate electrolyte</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Liu, Wei; Chen, Zidong; Zhang, Zheng; Jiang, Pingxian; Chen, Yungui; Paek, Eunsu; Wang, Yixian; Mitlin, David</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Environmental Science</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium metal battery (SMB, NMB) anodes can become dendritic due to an electrochem. unstable native Na-based solid electrolyte interphase (SEI).  Herein Li-ion activated tin sulfide graphene nanocomposite membrane (A-SnS-G) is employed as an artificial SEI layer, allowing cyclability of record-thin 100μm Na metal foils.  The thin Na metal is prepd. by a self-designed metallurgical rolling protocol.  A-SnS-G is initially placed onto the polypropylene (PP) separator but becomes in situ transferred onto the Na metal surface.  Accumulated capacity of 1000 mA h cm-2 is obtained after 500 cycles at 4 mA cm-2, with accumulated capacity-to-foil capacity (A/F) ratio of 90.9.  This is among the most favorable cycle life, accumulated capacity, and anode utilization combinations reported.  Protection by non-activated SnS-G membrane yields significantly worse cycling, albeit still superior to the baseline unprotected sodium.  Post-mortem and dedicated light optical anal. indicate that metal swelling, dendrite growth and dead metal formation is extensive for the unprotected sample, but is suppressed with A-SnS-G.  Per XPS, post-100 cycles near-surface structure of A-SnS-G is rich in metallic Sn alloys and inorg. carbonate salts.  As a proof of principle, an SMB with a high mass loading (6 mg cm-2) NVP cathode and a A-SnS-G protected anode delivered extended cyclability, achieving 74 mA h g-1 after 400 cycles at 0.4C.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref56"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref56" class="refNumLink">56</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit56" data-doi="10.1016/j.jpowsour.2007.06.043"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Naille, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dedryvère, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Martinez, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Leroy, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gonbeau, D.</span></span><span class="NLM_etal"></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">XPS Study of Electrode/Electrolyte Interfaces of H-Cu<sub>6</sub>Sn<sub>5</sub> Electrodes in Li-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>J.     </div><div class="casAbstract">The lithium-sulfur battery is receiving intense interest because its theoretical energy density exceeds that of lithium-ion batteries at much lower cost, but practical applications are still hindered by capacity decay caused by the polysulfide shuttle.  Here we report a strategy to entrap polysulfides in the cathode that relies on a chemical process, whereby a host--manganese dioxide nanosheets serve as the prototype--reacts with initially formed lithium polysulfides to form surface-bound intermediates.  These function as a redox shuttle to catenate and bind 'higher' polysulfides, and convert them on reduction to insoluble lithium sulfide via disproportionation.  The sulfur/manganese dioxide nanosheet composite with 75 wt% sulfur exhibits a reversible capacity of 1,300 mA h g(-1) at moderate rates and a fade rate over 2,000 cycles of 0.036%/cycle, among the best reported to date.
