TiO2 (anatase) was studied over a dimensional regime where both capacitive and Li intercalation processes contribute to the total stored charge.  An anal. of the voltammetric sweep data was used to distinguish between the amt. of charge stored by these 2 processes. SnSbSx/PCNF composite exhibits a unique two-dimensional nano-sheet morphol.                         cas_cit64R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">64</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Self-assembled flower-like NiFe2O4 decorated on 2D graphene nanosheets composite and their excellent electrochemical performance as anode materials for LIBs</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Chen, Xuefang; Huang, Ying; Zhang, Kaichuang; Feng, Xuansheng; Li, Suping</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Alloys and Compounds</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">686</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">905-913</span>CODEN:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier B.V.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Flower-like NiFe2O4 decorated on 2D graphene nanosheets composite and spherical NiFe2O4 decorated on 2D graphene nanosheets composite have been synthesized through an easy method including one-pot hydrothermal route and one-pot heat treatment, resp.  In the well-designed 3D dimensional system, 2D graphene nanosheets work as conductive matrix to support the flower-like NiFe2O4 and spherical NiFe2O4.  The structure integrates the advantage of graphene nanosheets to preserve the electrode activity and integrity, improving the electrochem. performance.  Lithium storage properties of the two kind of composites are compared as anode materials.  The results presented that flower-like NiFe2O4/graphene composite shows better electrochem. performance and cycling stability.  The first discharge capacity is 2632.6 mA h/g at a high c.d. of 100 mA/g.  After 50 cycles, the reversible capacity is 606.2 mA h/g and the coulomb efficiency reaches at 98.86%.  With the excellent electrochem. properties, flower-like NiFe2O4 decorated on 2D graphene nanosheets could be a kind of promising potential candidate anode materials for LIBs.  This research offers possibility to improve the lithium ion storage capacity of transition metal oxides through advisablely controlling the architecture and compn. of anode materials.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref65"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref65" class="refNumLink">65</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit65" data-doi="10.1039/C9TA02107H"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jin, Q.                         cas_cit65R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">65</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Experimental design and theoretical calculation for sulfur-doped carbon nanofibers as a high performance sodium-ion battery anode</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Jin, Qianzheng; Li, Wei; Wang, Kangli; Feng, Pingyuan; Li, Haomiao; Gu, Tiantian; Zhou, Min; Wang, Wei; Cheng, Shijie; Jiang, Kai</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry A: Materials for Energy and Sustainability</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Hard carbon is one of the most promising anode materials for sodium ion batteries (SIBs) due to its low cost, high cond. and suitable potential; however, its application is hindered by its relatively low capacity, and unsatisfactory rate capability and cyclability.  Herein, we have reported a high performance SIB anode of S-doped interconnected carbon nanofibers (denoted as S-CNFs) that was directly derived from the industrial waste product bacterial cellulose, demonstrating great potential for practical application and sustainable development.  The S-CNFs present high reversible capacities of 460 mA h g-1 at 0.05 A g-1 and 255 mA h g-1 at 10 A g-1, and preserved a capacity of 310 mA h g-1 at 1 A g-1 after 1100 cycles.  Structural and electrochem. analyses revealed that multiple factors including the expanded (002) interlayer spacing, the electrochem. active -C-S-C- covalent bonds, the capacitive process induced by a large surface area and considerable defects as well as the stable structure assocd. with the cross-linked network contributed to their excellent performance.  Furthermore, the first principles evaluations confirmed the sodium-storage mechanism of sulfur doping, which not only improved the interlayer distance for the mobility of Na+ but also promoted the electronegativity as well as the electrochem. activity and increased the adsorption of Na+.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref66"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref66" class="refNumLink">66</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit66" data-doi="10.1039/C8TA04417A"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Meng, Q.                         cas_cit66R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">66</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Theoretical investigation of zirconium carbide MXenes as prospective high capacity anode materials for Na-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Meng, Qiangqiang; Ma, Jiale; Zhang, Yonghui; Li, Zhen; Hu, Alice; Kai, Ji-Jung; Fan, Jun</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry A: Materials for Energy and Sustainability</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Currently, MXenes have been identified as promising electrode material candidates because of their excellent energy storage and elec. cond.  In the present study, Na adsorptions on 2D MXenes of Zr2C, Zr3C2, Zr2CO2, and Zr3C2O2 are explored by d. functional theory (DFT) calcns.  We have found that Zr2CO2/Zr3C2O2 MXenes show metallic behavior after Na adsorption and exhibit a low diffusion barrier (0.29/0.32 eV) and high storage capacity (up to Zr2CO2Na4/Zr3C2O2Na4 stoichiometry) for Na-ion batteries (NIBs).  Zr2CO2/Zr3C2O2 MXenes also exhibit excellent properties such as good elec. cond., low diffusion barrier, decreased equil. open circuit voltage (OCV), and high theor. capacity of Na storage, due to which they can be used as promising anode materials for NIBs.  In addn., the phys. origin of adsorption behavior of Na atoms on Zr2CO2/Zr3C2O2 as well as on Ti3C4, Ti3C2O2 and Ti3C2S2 MXenes is studied.  Our results give insights into understanding the manner in which the theor. capacity can be efficiently tuned by charge transfer and lattice mismatch factors, which will be useful for designing other 2D anode materials for NIBs.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref67"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref67" class="refNumLink">67</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit67" data-doi="10.1002/adma.201601387"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gao, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Meng, Q.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Na3V2(PO4)3 (NVP) with an open NASICON structure has drawn worldwide attention as a potential cathode material for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) owing to its high theor. capacity.  However, the inherently poor electronic cond. of NVP severely restricts its electrochem. performance, particularly for rate capability and long cycle performance.  Herein, high performance NVP/C cathode (denoted as NVP/C-T) is demonstrated by designing and synthesizing three-dimensional (3D) hierarchical porous NVP architecture via a facile hydrothermal technique.  In this hierarchical porous structure, ultrathin NVP nanosheets capped with in-situ carbon layers are interlinked to form hierarchical pores, convenient nanochannels and 3D conductive carbon framework.  This delicate structure not only provides adequate void for the intimate contact between electrode/electrolyte, shortens ionic diffusion distances, ensures the ultrafast electron transfer but also strengthens the structural stability of electrode material.  The as-prepd. NVP/C-T cathode exhibits a high reversible initial capacity (114.8 mAh g-1 at 1 C approaching the theor. capacity), excellent rate performance (89.3 mAh g-1 at 60 C and 73.2 mAh g-1 at 80 C) and long life span (93.3 mAh g-1 after 8000 cycles at 20 C).  In addn., the electrochem. properties of sym. full cell constructed with NVP/C-T/ NVP/C-T are also studied and high initial charge capacity (101.8 mAh g-1 at 0.25 C) and high stability (70.1 mAh g-1 at 2 C after 200 cycles) are achieved.  Significantly, the design of the 3D hierarchical porous nanocrystal@C strategy and scalable synthesis method may pave a way to develop high performance SIBs.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref70"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref70" class="refNumLink">70</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit70" data-doi="10.1016/j.jpowsour.2018.12.018"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Didwal, P. N.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Verma, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Min, C. W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Park, C. J.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Synthesis of 3-Dimensional Interconnected Porous Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>@C Composite as a High-Performance Dual Electrode for Na-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>J. Power Sources</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2019</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>413</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">1</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">10</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1016/j.jpowsour.2018.12.018</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D413%26publication_year%3D2019%26pages%3D1-10%26journal%3DJ.%2BPower%2BSources%26author%3DP.%2BN.%2BDidwal%26author%3DR.%2BVerma%26author%3DC.%2BW.%2BMin%26author%3DC.%2BJ.%2BPark%26title%3DSynthesis%2Bof%2B3-Dimensional%2BInterconnected%2BPorous%2BNa3V2%2528PO4%25293%2540C%2BComposite%2Bas%2Ba%2BHigh-Performance%2BDual%2BElectrode%2Bfor%2BNa-Ion%2BBatteries%26doi%3D10.1016%252Fj.jpowsour.2018.12.018&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.1c17466&amp;doiOfLink=10.1016%2Fj.jpowsour.2018.12.018&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit70R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">70</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Synthesis of 3-dimensional interconnected porous Na3V2(PO4)3@C composite as a high-performance dual electrode for Na-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Didwal, Pravin N.; Verma, Rakesh; Min, Chan-Woo; Park, Chan-Jin</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Power Sources</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2019</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">413</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">1-10</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JPSODZ</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0378-7753</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier B.V.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Three-dimensional (3-D) interconnected porous Na3V2(PO4)3 coated with carbon (NVP@C) is synthesized by a simple modified sol-gel method.  When 15 wt% glucose is used as the carbon precursor, the obtained NVP@C15 composite exhibits excellent electrochem. performance as a cathode as well as an anode for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs).  As a cathode, the NVP@C15 electrode delivers a high capacity of 116.9 mAh g-1 at a rate of 1 C, which is close to its theor. capacity.  Even at a high rate of 20 C, the NVP@C15 electrode exhibits an initial reversible capacity of 99.2 mAh g-1 and a capacity retention of 77% after 6000 cycles.  As an anode, the NVP@C15 delivers an initial reversible capacity of 85.8 mAh g-1 at a rate of 1 C.  At higher rates of 10 and 20 C, a remarkably good cyclability, with a capacity retention of 76% over 4000 cycles and 62% over 5000 cycles, resp., is achieved.  Furthermore, the full cell, composed of two sym. NVP@C15 electrodes, exhibits an initial reversible capacity of 73 mAh g-1 at a rate of 1 C.  In addn., capacity retentions of 88% after 100 cycles and 61% after 500 cycles are obtained at rates of 1 C and 5 C, resp.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li></ol></div><script type="text/javascript" Mater. Chem. A</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2019</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>7</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">19332</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">19341</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1039/C9TA06418D</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit2R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">2</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">High-concentration ether-based electrolyte boosts the electrochemical performance of SnS2-reduced graphene oxide for K-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Xie, Junpeng; Zhu, Yongqian; Zhuang, Ning; Li, Xiaodan; Yuan, Xinran; Li, Jinliang; Hong, Guo; Mai, Wenjie</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry A: Materials for Energy and Sustainability</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2019</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">7</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">33</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">19332-19341</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JMCAET</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2050-7496</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">To meet the urgent demand for energy storage systems, K-ion batteries (KIBs), with low cost and comparable electrochem. performance, have become one of the most promising alternatives to Li-ion batteries.  In this study, nanocryst. SnS2 anchored to reduced graphene oxide (SnS2-RGO) is investigated using ether-based electrolytes.  A reversible specific capacity of 436 mA h g-1 at 100 mA g-1 after 50 cycles was obtained, as well as the reversible specific capacity of 311 mA h g-1 at 500 mA g-1 after 150 cycles, which are much better than those using ester-based electrolytes.  Interestingly, it was found that high-concn. ether-based electrolytes can remarkably suppress the growth of KxS needles, which is probably due to the electrostatic shielding effect induced by the high concn. of K ions.  This discovery should be important for K-ion storage and could shed light on the design of better KIBs from the perspective of electrolytes.  Furthermore, we also utilized ex situ XRD patterns to reveal the electrochem. reaction process and reaction intermediate products of SnS2-RGO with high-concn. ether-based electrolytes.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref3"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref3" class="rightTabRefNumLink">3</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit3" data-doi="10.1007/s12598-020-01623-x"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, C. P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xie, K. X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">He, J. P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, Q. P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ma, J. M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, Q. H.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">SnO<sub>2</sub> Quantum Dots Modified N-Doped Carbon as High-Performance Anode for Lithium Ion Batteries by Enhanced Pseudocapacitance</span>. <i>Rare Met.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2021</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>40</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">48</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">56</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1007/s12598-020-01623-x</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="

        </div><div class="casAbstract">SnO2 is considered to be a promising candidate as anode material for lithium ion batteries, due to its high theor. specific capacity (1494 mAh.g-1).  Nevertheless, SnO2-based anodes suffer from poor electronic cond. and serious vol. variation (300%) during lithiation/delithiation process, leading to fast capacity fading.  To solve these problems, SnO2quantum dots modified N-doped carbon spheres (SnO2QDs@N-C) are fabricated by facile hydrolysis process of SnCl2, accompanied with the polymn. of polypyrrole (PPy), followed by a calcination method.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have attracted great attention recently due to the abundance of sodium resources, particularly for large-scale elec. energy storage applications for renewable energy and smart grids.  More and more nanostructured anode materials have been developed with the aims of high energy d., high cycling stability, and excellent rate capability, in which two-dimensional (2D) nanostructures are showing promise due to their shortened paths for sodium ion transportation and larger surface areas for sodium ion absorption.  Moreover, 2D materials (e.g. graphene) have been proved to be excellent supporting and conducting agents in SIB anodes due to their high elec. cond. and structural stability, in which synergetic effects between the graphene and the active materials are generally obsd.  This review is devoted to the recent progress in the use of 2D active materials and in composites consisting of both 2D supports and active materials as anodes for SIBs.  Based on the manner of sodium storage, their electrochem. performance for sodium storage is discussed in terms of four classifications, including carbonaceous materials (graphene and carbon nanosheets), alloy based materials (Sn, Sb, and P), conversion materials (phosphides/oxides/sulfides/selenides), and intercalation materials (Ti-based compds.). Moreover, the incorporation of the stable SnS2 crystal structure within the anode results in the superior discharge capacity of 527 mAh g-1 under a c.d. of 20 mA g-1 upon 50 cycles.  It maintains 340 mAh g-1 even the c.d. is increased to 800 mA g-1.  Aiming to further systematically study mechanism of composite with improved SIB performance, we construct the corresponding models based on exptl. data and conduct first-principles calcns.  The calcd. results indicate the sulfur atoms doped in GAs show a strong bridging effect with the SnS2 nanocrystals, contributing to build robust architecture for electrode.  Simultaneously, heteroatom dual doping of GAs shows the imperative function for improved elec. cond.  Herein, first-principles calcns. present a theor. explanation for outstanding cycling properties of SnS2@N,S-GA composite.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref12"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref12" class="rightTabRefNumLink">12</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit12" data-doi="10.1002/cssc.201900719"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sun, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ci, L.</span></span><span class="NLM_etal"></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Surface-Confined SnS<sub>2</sub>@C@R<sub>g</sub>O as High-Performance Anode Materials for Sodium- and Potassium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>ChemSusChem</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2019</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>12</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">2689</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">2700</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Two kinds of graphene-sulfur composites with 50 wt% of sulfur are prepd. using hydrothermal method and thermal mixing, resp.  Transmission Electron Microscopy (TEM) and Energy Dispersive X-ray Spectra mapping show that sulfur nanocrystals with size of ∼5 nm dispersed on graphene sheets homogeneously for the sample prepd. by hydrothermal method (NanoS@G).  While for the thermal mixed graphene-sulfur composite (S-G mixt.), sulfur shows larger and uneven size (50-200 nm).  X-ray Photoelectron Spectra (XPS) reveals the strong chem. bonding between the sulfur nanocrystals and graphene.  Comparing with the S-G mixt., the NanoS@G composite shows highly improved electrochem. performance as cathode for lithium-sulfur (Li-S) battery.  The NanoS@G composite delivers an initial capacity of 1400 mAh g-1 with the sulfur utilization of 83.7% at a c.d. of 335 mA g-1.  The capacity keeps above 720 mAh g-1 over 100 cycles.  The strong adherence of the sulfur nanocrystals on graphene immobilizes sulfur and polysulfides species and suppressed the "shuttle effect", resulting higher coulombic efficiency and better capacity retention.  Electrochem. impedance also suggests that the strong bonding enabled rapid electronic/ionic transport and improved electrochem. kinetics, therefore good rate capability is obtained.  These results demonstrate that the NanoS@G composite is a very promising candidate for high-performance Li-S batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref24"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref24" class="rightTabRefNumLink">24</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit24" data-doi="10.1039/C6CS00776G"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hwang, J.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Energy prodn. and storage technologies have attracted a great deal of attention for day-to-day applications.  In recent decades, advances in lithium-ion battery (LIB) technol. have improved living conditions around the globe.  LIBs are used in most mobile electronic devices as well as in zero-emission electronic vehicles.  However, there are increasing concerns regarding load leveling of renewable energy sources and the smart grid as well as the sustainability of lithium sources due to their limited availability and consequent expected price increase.  Therefore, whether LIBs alone can satisfy the rising demand for small- and/or mid-to-large-format energy storage applications remains unclear.  To mitigate these issues, recent research has focused on alternative energy storage systems.  Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are considered as the best candidate power sources because sodium is widely available and exhibits similar chem. to that of LIBs; therefore, SIBs are promising next-generation alternatives.  Recently, sodiated layer transition metal oxides, phosphates and org. compds. have been introduced as cathode materials for SIBs.  Simultaneously, recent developments have been facilitated by the use of select carbonaceous materials, transition metal oxides (or sulfides), and intermetallic and org. compds. as anodes for SIBs.  Apart from electrode materials, suitable electrolytes, additives, and binders are equally important for the development of practical SIBs.  Despite developments in electrode materials and other components, there remain several challenges, including cell design and electrode balancing, in the application of sodium ion cells.  In this article, we summarize and discuss current research on materials and propose future directions for SIBs.  This will provide important insights into scientific and practical issues in the development of SIBs.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref25"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref25" class="rightTabRefNumLink">25</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit25" data-doi="10.1016/j.electacta.2018.08.122"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, W.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium metal battery (SMB, NMB) anodes can become dendritic due to an electrochem. unstable native Na-based solid electrolyte interphase (SEI).  Herein Li-ion activated tin sulfide graphene nanocomposite membrane (A-SnS-G) is employed as an artificial SEI layer, allowing cyclability of record-thin 100μm Na metal foils.  The thin Na metal is prepd. by a self-designed metallurgical rolling protocol.  A-SnS-G is initially placed onto the polypropylene (PP) separator but becomes in situ transferred onto the Na metal surface.  Accumulated capacity of 1000 mA h cm-2 is obtained after 500 cycles at 4 mA cm-2, with accumulated capacity-to-foil capacity (A/F) ratio of 90.9.  This is among the most favorable cycle life, accumulated capacity, and anode utilization combinations reported.  Protection by non-activated SnS-G membrane yields significantly worse cycling, albeit still superior to the baseline unprotected sodium.  Post-mortem and dedicated light optical anal. indicate that metal swelling, dendrite growth and dead metal formation is extensive for the unprotected sample, but is suppressed with A-SnS-G.  Per XPS, post-100 cycles near-surface structure of A-SnS-G is rich in metallic Sn alloys and inorg. carbonate salts.  As a proof of principle, an SMB with a high mass loading (6 mg cm-2) NVP cathode and a A-SnS-G protected anode delivered extended cyclability, achieving 74 mA h g-1 after 400 cycles at 0.4C.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref56"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref56" class="rightTabRefNumLink">56</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit56" data-doi="10.1016/j.jpowsour.2007.06.043"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Naille, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dedryvère, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Martinez, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Leroy, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gonbeau, D.</span></span><span class="NLM_etal"></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">XPS Study of Electrode/Electrolyte Interfaces of H-Cu<sub>6</sub>Sn<sub>5</sub> Electrodes in Li-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>J.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Considering that the high capacity, long-term cycle life, and high-rate capability of anode materials for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) is a bottleneck currently, a series of Co-doped FeS2 solid solns. with different Co contents were prepd. by a facile solvothermal method, and for the first time their Na-storage properties were investigated.     </div><div class="casAbstract">The lithium-sulfur battery is receiving intense interest because its theoretical energy density exceeds that of lithium-ion batteries at much lower cost, but practical applications are still hindered by capacity decay caused by the polysulfide shuttle.  Here we report a strategy to entrap polysulfides in the cathode that relies on a chemical process, whereby a host--manganese dioxide nanosheets serve as the prototype--reacts with initially formed lithium polysulfides to form surface-bound intermediates.  These function as a redox shuttle to catenate and bind 'higher' polysulfides, and convert them on reduction to insoluble lithium sulfide via disproportionation.  The sulfur/manganese dioxide nanosheet composite with 75 wt% sulfur exhibits a reversible capacity of 1,300 mA h g(-1) at moderate rates and a fade rate over 2,000 cycles of 0.036%/cycle, among the best reported to date.  We furthermore show that this mechanism extends to graphene oxide and suggest it can be employed more widely.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref61"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref61" class="rightTabRefNumLink">61</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit61" data-doi="10.1021/jp074464w"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Polleux, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lim, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dunn, B.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Pseudocapacitive Contributions to Electrochemical Energy Storage in TiO<sub>2</sub> (Anatase) Nanoparticles</span>. <i>J.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The advantages of using nanostructured materials for electrochem. energy storage have focused on the benefits assocd. with short path lengths.  However, another contribution, capacitive effects which become increasingly important at nanoscale dimensions, was studied.  Nanocryst. TiO2 (anatase) was studied over a dimensional regime where both capacitive and Li intercalation processes contribute to the total stored charge.  An anal. of the voltammetric sweep data was used to distinguish between the amt. of charge stored by these 2 processes.  At particle sizes &lt;10 nm, capacitive contributions became increasingly important, leading to greater amts. of total stored charge (gravimetrically normalized) with decreasing TiO2 particle size.  The area normalized capacitance is well &gt;100 μF/cm2, confirming that the capacitive contribution was pseudocapacitive.  Also, decreasing the particle size to the nanoscale regime led to faster charge/discharge rates because diffusion-controlled Li ion intercalation was replaced by faradaic reactions which occur at the surface of the material. L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chang, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">He, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, Q. X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhao, H. J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Huang, X. J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gao, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tang, Z. Y.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Co<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> Hexagonal Platelets with Controllable Facets Enabling Highly Efficient Visible-Light Photocatalytic Reduction of Co<sub>2</sub></span>. <i>Adv. Mater.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2016</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>28</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">6485</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">6490</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1002/adma.201601387</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit67R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">67</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Co3O4 Hexagonal Platelets with Controllable Facets Enabling Highly Efficient Visible-Light Photocatalytic Reduction of CO2</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Gao, Chao; Meng, Qiangqiang; Zhao, Kun; Yin, Huajie; Wang, Dawei; Guo, Jun; Zhao, Shenlong; Chang, Lin; He, Meng; Li, Qunxiang; Zhao, Huijun; Huang, Xingjiu; Gao, Yan; Tang, Zhiyong</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Materials (Weinheim, Germany)</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">28</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">30</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">6485-6490</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ADVMEW</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0935-9648</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Uniform Co3O4 hexagonal platelets with exposed {112} facets are demonstrated as excellent heterogeneous catalysts for photocatalytic CO2 redn. using Ru(bpy)3Cl2 as a photosensitizer.  The exposed {112} facets of Co3O4 platelets favor adsorption of CO2 and subsequent redn. to CO via COOH, which accounts for the first discovery of the facet effect of cobalt oxides on photocatalytic CO2 redn.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref68"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref68" class="rightTabRefNumLink">68</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit68" data-doi="10.1002/eem2.12028"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jiang, Y. Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, J. P.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Definitions of Pseudocapacitive Materials: A Brief Review</span>. <i>Energy Environ. Mater.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2019</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>2</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">30</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">37</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1002/eem2.12028</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord empty" id="
                            rightTab-cas_R
                        "><div class="casContent">There is no corresponding record for this reference.</div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref69"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref69" class="rightTabRefNumLink">69</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit69" data-doi="10.1016/j.cej.2018.07.118"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ling, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cai, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xie, D. L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, M. J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lin, Y. S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jiang, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Shen, K. E.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xiong, K. Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sun, X. H.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Three-Dimensional Hierarchical Porous Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>/C Structure with High Rate Capability and Cycling Stability for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Chem. Eng. J.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2018</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>353</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">264</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">272</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1016/j.cej.2018.07.118</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit69R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">69</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Three-dimensional hierarchical porous Na3V2(PO4)3/C structure with high rate capability and cycling stability for sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Ling, Rui; Cai, Shu; Xie, Dongli; Li, Xin; Wang, Mingjing; Lin, Yishu; Jiang, Song; Shen, Kaier; Xiong, Kunzhou; Sun, Xiaohong</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemical Engineering Journal (Amsterdam, Netherlands)</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2018</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">353</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">264-272</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">CMEJAJ</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1385-8947</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier B.V.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Na3V2(PO4)3 (NVP) with an open NASICON structure has drawn worldwide attention as a potential cathode material for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) owing to its high theor. capacity.  However, the inherently poor electronic cond. of NVP severely restricts its electrochem. performance, particularly for rate capability and long cycle performance.  Herein, high performance NVP/C cathode (denoted as NVP/C-T) is demonstrated by designing and synthesizing three-dimensional (3D) hierarchical porous NVP architecture via a facile hydrothermal technique.  In this hierarchical porous structure, ultrathin NVP nanosheets capped with in-situ carbon layers are interlinked to form hierarchical pores, convenient nanochannels and 3D conductive carbon framework.  This delicate structure not only provides adequate void for the intimate contact between electrode/electrolyte, shortens ionic diffusion distances, ensures the ultrafast electron transfer but also strengthens the structural stability of electrode material.  The as-prepd. NVP/C-T cathode exhibits a high reversible initial capacity (114.8 mAh g-1 at 1 C approaching the theor. capacity), excellent rate performance (89.3 mAh g-1 at 60 C and 73.2 mAh g-1 at 80 C) and long life span (93.3 mAh g-1 after 8000 cycles at 20 C).  In addn., the electrochem. properties of sym. full cell constructed with NVP/C-T/ NVP/C-T are also studied and high initial charge capacity (101.8 mAh g-1 at 0.25 C) and high stability (70.1 mAh g-1 at 2 C after 200 cycles) are achieved.  Significantly, the design of the 3D hierarchical porous nanocrystal@C strategy and scalable synthesis method may pave a way to develop high performance SIBs.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref70"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref70" class="rightTabRefNumLink">70</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit70" data-doi="10.1016/j.jpowsour.2018.12.018"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Didwal, P. N.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Verma, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Min, C. W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Park, C. J.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Synthesis of 3-Dimensional Interconnected Porous Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>@C Composite as a High-Performance Dual Electrode for Na-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>J. Power Sources</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2019</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>413</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">1</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">10</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1016/j.jpowsour.2018.12.018</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit70R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">70</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Synthesis of 3-dimensional interconnected porous Na3V2(PO4)3@C composite as a high-performance dual electrode for Na-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Didwal, Pravin N.; Verma, Rakesh; Min, Chan-Woo; Park, Chan-Jin</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Power Sources</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2019</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">413</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">1-10</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JPSODZ</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0378-7753</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier B.V.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Three-dimensional (3-D) interconnected porous Na3V2(PO4)3 coated with carbon (NVP@C) is synthesized by a simple modified sol-gel method.  When 15 wt% glucose is used as the carbon precursor, the obtained NVP@C15 composite exhibits excellent electrochem. performance as a cathode as well as an anode for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs).  As a cathode, the NVP@C15 electrode delivers a high capacity of 116.9 mAh g-1 at a rate of 1 C, which is close to its theor. capacity.  Even at a high rate of 20 C, the NVP@C15 electrode exhibits an initial reversible capacity of 99.2 mAh g-1 and a capacity retention of 77% after 6000 cycles.  As an anode, the NVP@C15 delivers an initial reversible capacity of 85.8 mAh g-1 at a rate of 1 C.  At higher rates of 10 and 20 C, a remarkably good cyclability, with a capacity retention of 76% over 4000 cycles and 62% over 5000 cycles, resp., is achieved.  Furthermore, the full cell, composed of two sym. NVP@C15 electrodes, exhibits an initial reversible capacity of 73 mAh g-1 at a rate of 1 C.  In addn., capacity retentions of 88% after 100 cycles and 61% after 500 cycles are obtained at rates of 1 C and 5 C, resp.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li></ol></li><li id="pane-m3" aria-labelledby="pane-m3" class="tab__pane tab__pane-suppInfo"><h2 class="supp-info-label">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article_supporting-info" id="rightTab-notes-1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="rightTab-_i19">Supporting Information</h2></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge at <a href="/doi/10.1021/acsami.1c17466?goto=supporting-info" class="ext-link">https://pubs.acs.org/doi/10.1021/acsami.1c17466</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">XRD patterns of the as-prepared samples; XPS spectra of survey spectra, the S/Sn atomic ratio, and Co atomic ratio, S 2p, C 1s, Sn 3d, and Co 2p for the samples; EDS test of the 7% Co-SnS<sub>2</sub>/S@r-G sample; Raman shift curves of full spectra; BET curve of the as-prepared samples; SEM, TEM, and HRTEM images of the as-prepared samples; cycling performance of the electrodes at a rate of 200 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, 1 A g<sup>–1</sup>, and 2 A g<sup>–1</sup>; CV profiles of the as-prepared electrodes under 0.2 mV s<sup>–1</sup>; CV curves of 7% Co-SnS<sub>2</sub>/S@r-G, and SnS<sub>2</sub>/S@r-G electrodes at different scan rates; relationship between the logarithm anodic peak current and logarithm scan rates; Nyquist plots for the electrodes; SEM and HRTEM of 7% Co-SnS<sub>2</sub>/S@r-G electrodes after 50 and 200 discharge/charge cycles; possible Na diffusion pathways and diffusion barrier profiles of the second layer on the SnS<sub>2</sub> and Co-doped SnS<sub>2</sub> system; various Na<sup>+</sup> adsorption configurations of Co-doped SnS<sub>2</sub> in their top views; XRD, SEM, and cycling performance of porous Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>@MWCNTs (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c17466/suppl_file/am1c17466_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c17466/suppl_file/am1c17466_si_001.pdf">am1c17466_si_001.pdf (3.13 MB)</a></li></ul></div></div><div id="rightTab-figshare-widget"></div><br><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzoneRight" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzoneRight"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="b84e6b6c-b4d0-4427-b286-3cf14d52d013" class="mb-4">

	    }

	});

	});

	});

	}); 



})();

<script nonce="9318812cfb2254b8-SJC">
document.addEventListener('DOMContentLoaded', function () {
    const modalContainer = document.querySelector('.my-main-container');

    // Function to show the modal
    function showModal() {
        modalContainer.style.display = 'flex';
        document.querySelector('.overlay').style.display = 'block';
    }

    // Function to hide the modal when the close button is clicked
    function hideModal() {
        modalContainer.style.display = 'none';
        document.querySelector('.overlay').style.display = 'none';
    }

    // Hide the modal initially
    hideModal();

    // Function to handle clicks outside the modal
    function handleClickOutside(event) {
        // const modalContainer = document.querySelector(".my-main-container");
        // Check if the click target is not a child of the modal container
        if (!modalContainer.contains(event.target)) {
            hideModal();
        }
    }

    // Add a click event listener to the close button
    document
        .querySelector('.close-button')
        .addEventListener('click', hideModal);

    // Add a click event listener to the document to handle clicks outside the modal
    document.addEventListener('click', handleClickOutside);

    const screenHeight = window.innerHeight;
    function updateModalPosition() {
        const scrollY = window.scrollY;
        // console.log("scrollY= " + scrollY);
        // modalContainer.style.top = `${50 + scrollY}px`;
        modalContainer.style.top = `${scrollY + screenHeight / 2}px`;
        // console.log("new top= " + modalContainer.style.top);
    }

    // Update the modal position when the user scrolls
    window.addEventListener('scroll', updateModalPosition);

    // Initialize the modal position
    updateModalPosition();

    //---Cookie---
    // Function to set the cookie with a given name, value, and expiration time in days
    function setCookie(name, value, days) {
        var expires = '';
        if (days) {
            var date = new Date();
            date.setTime(date.getTime() + days * 24 * 60 * 60 * 1000);
            expires = '; expires=' + date.toUTCString();
            console.log(expires + 'this is expires dateUTC');
        }
        document.cookie = name + '=' + (value || '') + expires + '; path=/';
        // console.log("document.cookie is: " + document.cookie);
    }

    if (!document.cookie.includes('modalAd')) {
        // Show Modal
        showModal();

        // Set the cookie with the name "modalAd", value "true", and expiration of 3 day
        setCookie('modalAd', 'true', 3);
    } else {
        hideModal();
    }
});

</script>
<link rel="stylesheet" type="text/css" href="/pb-assets/smartgroup_ads/Modal-LeadGen-1691422642793.css">
            
        </div>

<script nonce="9318812cfb2254b8-SJC">
    document.addEventListener("DOMContentLoaded", function(){
    // Function to get a Human readable formatted date
    function getFormattedDateForMaintenance() {
        let today = new Date();
        let dd = String(today.getDate()).padStart(2, '0');
        //January is 0
        let mm = String(today.getMonth() + 1).padStart(2, '0'); 
        let yyyy = today.getFullYear();
        
        let daysOfWeek = ['Sunday', 'Monday', 'Tuesday', 'Wednesday', 'Thursday', 'Friday', 'Saturday'];
        let monthsOfYear = ['January', 'February', 'March', 'April', 'May', 'June', 'July', 'August', 'September', 'October', 'November', 'December'];

        let dayOfWeek = daysOfWeek[today.getDay()];
        let monthOfYear = monthsOfYear[today.getMonth()];

        return dayOfWeek + ', ' + monthOfYear + ' ' + dd + ', ' + yyyy;
    }
    
    // Insert the date into the .system-message-date element
    document.querySelector('.system-message-date').innerText = getFormattedDateForMaintenance();
});
</script>

        errormessage =

      function init() {
        setTimeout(function () {
          var cookieExist = getCookie("gdpr");
          if (cookieExist) {
            //console.log("gdpr cookie exist");
            return false;
          } else {
            //console.log("gdpr cookie does not exist")
            gdprMessage();
          }
          //Events
          $("#closebtn").on("click", hideForm);

          //Handle submit
          $("#gdpr-message-btn").on("click", function (event) {
            handleClick();
          });

          // $(document).click(function(event) {
          // 	event.stopPropagation();
          //   console.log(event.target);
          //   if (!$(event.target).closest("#gdpr-message-content").length) {

            setCookie("gdpr", "true");
            hideForm();
          });

      function gdprMessage() {
        //console.log("Show GDPR Message");
        var tMessage, emailInput, messageButton, ewrap;

        $("#gdpr-message-modal").show();
      }

      function handleClick() {
        //console.log($("#gdpr-input").val());
        emailValid = validateEmail($("#gdpr-input").val());
        if (emailValid) {
          //Submit the form
          //Hide form
          if ($(".gdpr_err")) {
            $(".gdpr_err").hide();
          }

          $("#gdpr-message-content");
          //If successful then...
          setCookie("gdpr", "true");
          //console.log("emailValid " + emailValid + " submiting form");

          //Show error message
          gdprErrorMessage();
          //console.log("emailValid " + emailValid);
        }
      }

      function gdprErrorMessage() {
        if (errorStatus) {
          return false;
        } else {
          errorStatus = true;
          $("#gdpr-message-content").append(errormessage);
        }
      }

      function validateEmail(email) {
        var re =
          /^(([^<>()\[\]\\.,;:\s@"]+(\.[^<>()\[\]\\.,;:\s@"]+)*)|(".+"))@((\[[0-9]{1,3}\.[0-9]{1,3}\.[0-9]{1,3}\.[0-9]{1,3}\])|(([a-zA-Z\-0-9]+\.)+[a-zA-Z]{2,}))$/;
        return re.test(String(email).toLowerCase());
      }

      function setCookie(name, value) {
        var expires = "";
        expires = "; expires=Fri, 31 Dec 9999 23:59:59 GMT";
        document.cookie = name + "=" + (value || "") + expires + "; path=/";
      }

      function getCookie(name) {
        var match = document.cookie.match(new RegExp(name + "=([^;]+)"));
        if (match) return match[1];
      }

      function hideForm() {
        //And set cookie
        $("#gdpr-message-modal").hide();
        setCookie("gdpr", "false");
        gtag("event", "GDPR Modal", {
          event_category: "Pubs GDPR",
          event_label: "GDPR Dismissal",
        });
      }

      return {
        init: init,
      };
    })();

  </script>

        <script nonce="9318812cfb2254b8-SJC">
var currentJournal = "ACS Applied Materials &amp;amp; Interfaces";
$(".citationLinks > a").click(function(e){
    if($(this).text() === "ACS Full Text ") {
        gtag('event', 'ACS Full Text', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "Crossref") {
        gtag('event', 'Crossref', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "CAS") {
        gtag('event', 'CAS', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "PubMed") {
        gtag('event', 'PubMed', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "Google Scholar") {
        gtag('event', 'Google Scholar', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).hasClass("issue-item_open-url") === true) {
        gtag('event', 'OpenURL', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
});
</script>
